==PAGE:2==
Abstract
More than a century ago was discovered the separation of air into into its primary
components - Oxygen, Nitrogen, Argon and other minor air components - which never
ceased to find new applications in multiples fields of the society, always leading to greater
production requirements. In particular, Oxygen finds a lot of applications in industry
and medical sector, whereas Nitrogen became an essential compound for a wide range
of industrial and chemical processes, and forming ammonia when bounded with three
Hydrogen atoms, became vital for agriculture, as is it employed as fertilizers. The current
air separation processes require a lot energy, often provided using fossil sources and hence
associated to large greenhouse gases emissions. In this paper, a techno-economic analysis
of the main air separation technologies was realized to assess the competitiveness of a
green Nitrogen production process based on an electrochemical membrane. The costs and
energy needs of the current air separation technologies were evaluated for the production
of N2 enriched, pure, and pure & compressed gaseous streams, intended for the synthesis
of green ammonia. The optimal combination of electricity supply and electrolyzers for
the production of green H2 for the combustion of remaining O2 traces were evaluated
for 2020 investment and 2030/2050 projections. The energy needs of the electrochemical
process were based on experimental results, while the investment costs were based on cell
manufacturing volumes (similarly as done for SOEC capital costs calculations, and the
operation costs on typical operational costs calculations, accounting for energy consumption,
maintenance, stack replacements, etc. The techno-economic analysis eventually shows
that despite relatively high energy requirements for the production of purified Nitrogen
stream (0.3530 kWh per kg of 98.73%mol N2/1.27%mol Ar in 2020), from a penetration
of the ”N2 to NH3 market” of 1.6% (6087 ton per day of pure N2), electrochemical oxygen
pumping plants could compete with the cheapest identified air separation technologies
(Cryogenic), by reaching levelized costs of 0.015$2020 per kg of purified Nitrogen stream
(still containing Argon) in 2030, down to 0.11 $2020 per kg of purified Nitrogen stream for
a 2050 investment.

1
Introduction
1.1
Air separation process: applications and demands
The main objective of an air separation unit (ASU) is to separate the primary components
of atmospheric air which consists of 78.08% of Nitrogen, 20.95% of Oxygen, 0.93% of
Argon, 0.0018% of Neon, 0.0005 % of Helium, 0.00011% of Krypton and 0.000009% of
Xenon.
Air separation was discovered for more than a hundred years ago, and separation pro-
cesses have never ceased to be developed and improved since then. Air separation is
currently achieved by the following three main current technologies: Cryogenic Distillation,
Pressure Swing Adsorption (PSA) and membrane based ASU. These different technolo-
gies rely on different phenomena and operate under specific conditions of pressure and
temperature, leading to different volumes and qualities of production (purity, state and
final pressure). The different air separation technologies are presented in the next Section 2.
Nitrogen is a non-toxic and non-flammable gas, non-reactive with most other gases at room
temperature and pressure, and is hence considered as an inert gas. Nitrogen is produced
in a huge amount every year and is used in various industry processes and applications,
such as in the food and beverage industry for the cooling, the freezing, and for packaging,
storage and transport, and in the chemical industry for various applications such as the
production of fertilizers, ammonia, and other chemicals-and in the electronic industry as a
purge gas; a shielding gas for welding; and in the medical industry for cryotherapy and
medical devices and procedures; in the automotive industry and for scientific research in
general.
As Nitrogen, Oxygen is also widely used in various industries: medical applications; welding
and cutting, aerospace industry, water treatment, metal refining, laboratory and research,
aquaculture, and food and beverage industries for the main ones.
According to an IEA report on Ammonia Technology Roadmap [16], the world Nitrogen
demand could increase from around 150 Mt Nitrogen in 2020 to 178 Mt according to a
Stated Policies or a Sustainable Development Scenario by 2030, or up to 244 Mt by 2030
in a Net Zero Emission by 2050 scenario. The yearly demands are predicted to reach 208
Mt, 344 Mt and up to 460 Mt Nitrogen by 2050 respectively for the three cited scenarios.
1.2
Ammonia current production and future demands
According to the IEA ([16]), ammonia is currently mainly used for the production of
fertilisers, industrial applications and as an intermediate for other chemical products.
Ammonia is almost only produced through the Haber-Bosch process, invented in the
early 20th and is energy intensive, and the hydrogen required for the NH3 molecules to
be synthesized is essentially produced from fossil energy sources, and as stated in the
==PAGE:8==
1
Introduction
IEA report ([16]): ”In 2020, of the 185 Mt of ammonia produced, 72% relied on natural
gas-based steam reforming, 26% on coal gasification, about 1% on oil products, and a
fraction of a percentage point on electrolysis”. Hence ammonia production processes are
very energy consuming and currently lead to large CO2 emissions (450 Mt CO2 in 2020,
representing 5% of the industrial CO2 emissions).
However, ammonia is also considered as a potential energy carrier for the future, and
according to [16] and the scenarios considered, while ammonia demand reached 185 MT in
2020 for conventional uses, the demand for ammonia could reach around 420 Mt in 2050
according to the Sustainable Development Scenario, with 220 Mt of ammonia required
for the existing uses, and around 140 Mt and 60 MT for the Maritime fuel and Power
generation sectors respectively. Considering a 2050 Net Zero Emissions Scenario, the
Maritime fuel and Power generation sectors ammonia demand could rise up to 260 MT
and 80 Mt respectively, for a global demand of 560 Mt of ammonia. To be considered as
an energy carrier in a decarbonized future, ammonia must be produced through non or
less emissive and energy consuming processes. This induces green production of Hydrogen
and Nitrogen, but also a less energy consuming and less CO2 emissive process than the
Haber-Bosch process, for which the combustion of fossil fuels is often used to fulfill the
energy requirements. Alternatives to the Haber-Bosch process are already studied, with
an electrochemical Haber Bosch process is for instance [19] and the Solid State Ammonia
Synthesis (SSAS) ([6], [12]).
In alignment with these future green N2 and ammonia needs, the two main objectives
of this study are the techno-economic assessment (TEA) of the current Air Separation
Technologies and the characterization of an electrochemical Nitrogen enriched streams
production process by Oxygen ”pumping”, both from an energy-performance and an
economic point of view, leading to the comparison of this O2 pumping electrochemical
process with the current ASU technologies for the production of NES, pure N2 stream or
pure & compressed N2 stream, to be further used for NH3 synthesis.
1.3
Techno-Economic Assessment objectives
The first objective of the TEA is to obtain the energy and economic characteristics of the
three main ASU technologies from different literature references and production plants, in
order to form a base for an energy and economic comparison with the electrochemical O2
pump, regarding the characteristics of the produced N2 streams.
To go into detail, the TEA is leading to a comparison of the different technologies and
the electrochemical O2 pump, both from an economic and an energetic point of view,
regarding the production of:
• Nitrogen Enriched Streams (NES) with various N2 molar purity levels and composi-
tions, pressure levels, state (liquid or gaseous) and production rates ∗,
∗As the wide range of Nitrogen applications require different stream quality (state, composition and
pressure), is was still considered relevant to include the NES production comparison in this study.
Page 2 of 150
==PAGE:9==
1
Introduction
• Pure Gaseous Nitrogen Streams, in order to obtain a product than only differs by
its pressure, and hence allows a fair comparison between technologies,
• Pure & Compressed Gaseous Nitrogen Streams (up to 150 bar), as intended for use
in the synthesis of NH3, which also eventually provides an identical final product for
all the technologies, allowing the best comparison.
For each air separation technology and the electrochemical O2 pump, and for each of the
three considered Nitrogen streams (N2S), the lowest energy requirements and Levelized
Costs of Nitrogen (LCON) were assessed by combining electricity supply options and
electrolyzers technologies for 2020, 2030, or 2050 investments and over a 30-year economic
period.
1.4
Electrochemical O2 Pump Characterization objectives
The electrochemical O2 pump developed in DTU Energy laboratories was characterized in
this study, in order to assess its performances from an energy point of view in a first place,
but also to evaluate the costs of deployment of the technology if it were to be developed
at an industrial scale.
The experiments were performed with Lanthanum Strontium Iron/Cerium Gadolinium
Oxygen (LSF/CGO) cells with varying parameters:
• temperature (from 800 down to 500 ◦C)
• feed stream volumetric flow (only comprising N2 and O2!)
or utilization rate
(corresponding to the proportion of oxygen atoms to be extracted from the feed
stream against the total atoms initially present in the feed stream)
• sweep gas composition.
The best operating conditions were identified and the results analyzed to compare the
operating conditions (in particular the use of air or pure O2 as sweep gas) and to evaluate
the energy requirements for the different NES N2 molar purity levels to be reached.
A further purification step by combustion of the remaining oxygen traces in the NES
with H2 was considered, and an optimal intermediate NES purity level was identified to
minimize the energy requirements.
From the energy requirements and power density of the best results observed, the cell
surface requirements were calculated as a function of the production rates, allowing the
assessment of the investment costs using an approach similar to SOEC capital costs
estimations.
Page 3 of 150
==PAGE:10==
1
Introduction
From these performances assessment and economic considerations, it was finally possible
to compare the LSF/CGO electrochemical O2 pump technology presented in this study
and the current air separation technologies, both from an economic and an energetic point
of view regarding the comparison of the different N2S previously mentioned.

==PAGE:11==
2
Air Separation technologies for Nitrogen production
2
Air Separation technologies for Nitrogen produc-
tion
In this chapter, the general working principles and main characteristics of the three main
current ASU are presented, before a detailed presentation of the plants found in the
literature and used as references in the next section 3.
2.1
Cryogenic Air Distillation technology
Air separation and more precisely air distillation dates back to the late 19th when Carl
von Linde discovered that air could be liquefied [20], and its components separated thanks
to the difference in boiling points temperature (-183°C for Oxygen, -186°C for Argon and
-196°C for Nitrogen). Soon after the world’s first ASU for oxygen production was built, in
1902, and followed by the world’s first air separation plant for the recovery of nitrogen in
1904.
Nowadays, cryogenic distillation is the most common for O2 and N2 production, as the
plants can be built at huge scales and benefit from the economy of scale. As an example,
in 1997 the company Linde named after the inventor of the first process, has built and
installed five plants in Cantarell, Mexico, for the production of 10000 tonnes of Nitrogen
per day for each of the five plants, and built five cryogenic plants in Jamnagar (India)
in 2017, with an O2 production rate of 5250 tonnes per day for each of the plants. It is
currently the most commonly used technology, providing high purity products with high
production capacities, often associated to ammonia production on-site.
The separation principles of the cryogenic distillation have quickly evolved from a single-
column for oxygen production to a double-column rectification system allowing the simul-
taneous production of both oxygen and nitrogen. As explained in a review document of
Air separation plants released by Linde company [20], the double column configuration
involved ”installing a pressure column below the low-pressure column. At the top of this
pressure column, pure nitrogen was drawn off, liquefied in a condenser and fed to the
top low-pressure column as reflux. At the top of the low-pressure column, pure gaseous
nitrogen was withdrawn, while liquid oxygen evaporated at the bottom of this column to
deliver pure gaseous oxygen. This principle of double-column rectification combining the
condenser and evaporator to form a heat exchanger unit is still used today.” The double
column rectification also contains a common heat exchanger combining a condensed and
evaporator, leading to the separation of the rectification into two areas at different pressure
levels.
The main steps of a typical cryogenic air separation are also detailed in this document:
• Air compression:
– Compression of ambient air by a multi-stage turbo compressor with inter-coolers
at a supply pressure of approx. 6 bar,
Page 5 of 150
==PAGE:12==
2
Air Separation technologies for Nitrogen production
– Removal of dust particles by a mechanical air filter at the inlet of the compressor.
• Air cooling and purification:
– Cooling of process air with water in a direct contact cooler and removal of
water soluble air impurities,
– Chilling of cooling water in an evaporation cooler against dry nitrogen waste
gas from the rectification process,
– Removal of CO2, water and hydrocarbons from the process air in periodically
loaded/regenerated molecular sieve adsorbers.
• Cold production and internal product compression:
– Cooling of process air in heat exchangers down to nearly liquefaction temperature
by means of counter current with gas streams from the rectification process,
– Further compression of a sidestream of process air by an air booster compressor.
Expansion and cold production of the boosted air stream in an expansion
turbine,
– Expansion and liquefaction of a sidestream of the boosted air in a liquid
separator,
– Evaporation and warming to ambient temperature of the pumped oxygen and
nitrogen product in high-pressure heat exchangers.
• Cryogenic rectification of air:
– Pre-separation of the cooled and liquefied air within the pressure column into
oxygen-enriched liquid in the column sump and pure nitrogen gas at the column
top,
– Liquefaction of the pure nitrogen gas in the condenser/reboiler against boiling
oxygen in the sump of the low-pressure column. Liquefied nitrogen provides
the reflux for the pressure column and (after sub-cooling) for the low-pressure
column,
– Further separation of the oxygen-enriched liquid within the low pressure column
into pure oxygen in the sump and nitrogen waste gas at the top.
• Cryogenic rectification of Argon:
– Argon-enriched gas from the low-pressure column is transformed into oxygen-free
crude argon by means of separation within the crude argon column,
– Pumping back liquid oxygen from the crude argon column sump into the
low-pressure column. Removal of the remaining nitrogen in the pure argon
column.
Page 6 of 150
==PAGE:13==
2
Air Separation technologies for Nitrogen production
The products characteristics depend on the plant configuration and target (N2 or O2
priority), but a cryogenic distillation plant aiming at N2 production typically has a
production range between 250 and 50000 Nm3/h of NES ([33]) which corresponds to 7.5
to 1500 tpd of NES, with a very high product purity, in any case superior to 99% in molar
purity, and up to 99,999% purity (with Argon and/or O2 in the remaining impurities).
The pressure of the final products varies according to the product state (liquid or gaseous),
usually between 1 and 10 bar, but can reach high pressure ranges, as presented by Air
Liquide ([22]) with the production of liquid N2 and O2 respectively at pressure levels of
26 and 44 bar.
2.2
Pressure Swing Adsorption technology
The Pressure Swing Adsorption technology are usually favored for medium capacity and
purity of the products, and rely on the Adsorption process of specific gas molecules on a
surface when high pressure is applied, which results in attractive forces and a decrease of
the potential energy. Once the gas molecules to be separated for the main gas volume
(called the Heavy product) are bound to the surface , the rest of the gas, not bound to the
surface (called Light product) is ”flushed”, and the pressure is then lowered in order to
release the Heavy product molecules and restart the process with a new gas stream to treat.
The Stripping Reflux PSA (SR-PSA), patented by Charles Skarstrom in the 1960s revolu-
tionized the process by implementing two packed bed columns associated in a opposite
four-step cycle ([18]), where:
• The first bed successively undergoes:
– (1) A pressurization step,
– (2) An adsorption step,
– (3) A countercurrent blowdown step,
– (4) A coutercurrent purge step,
• While the second bed successively undergoes the following steps:
– (1) A countercurrent blowdown step,
– (2) A coutercurrent purge step,
– (3) A pressurization step,
– (4) An adsorption step.
The two beds operate in a opposite cycle, as one is highly pressurized and adsorbs the
heavy product molecules whereas the other bed is releasing the light product at lowered
pressure.
Using this process for Air Separation, the Light product would be the NES, while the Heavy
product would be Oxygen enriched air, but other processes have been developed, such as
Page 7 of 150
==PAGE:14==
2
Air Separation technologies for Nitrogen production
the Enriching Reflux PSA (ER-PSA) or the Dual-Reflux PSA (DR-PSA) in the 1990s,
using different adsorbent materials (carbon molecular sieve (CMS), zeolites, activated
carbon, etc.), cycles and operating conditions.
The PSA ASU usually produce NES with high N2 molar purity (¿98%) but can also
produce very high pure NES (¿99.9% mol N2), with a production rate of 3 to 3000 Nm3/h
of NES, corresponding to 0.09 to 90 tpd of NES, with a pressure up to 6-10 bar ([33]).
2.3
Membrane based technology
The last of the three main air separation technologies is the membrane-based separation,
which is slightly less mature than the PSA can cryogenic distillation, as the first mem-
brane based ASU was commercialized at an industrial-scale in the 1980s with the Permea
PRISM®([18]). The membrane based separation technology is also a pressure-driven
process, but relies on the permeation and selectivity characteristics of the the porous
membrane.
For the separation of N2 and O2, a pressurized stream of air is fed to a membrane
presenting a high O2 selectivity, meaning that the membrane allows much more easily the
passage of Oxygen than the passage or Nitrogen through the membrane.
The ”retentate” stream, corresponding to the stream and atoms that did not cross the
membrane, has a much higher Nitrogen purity due to the extraction of Oxygen molecules
from the feed stream, and still has a high pressure (sensibly the same as the feed stream
initial pressure).
On the other side, the ”permeate” side, constituted by the molecules that were extracted
from the feed stream and crossed the membrane, is much richer in Oxygen than the feed
stream, and has a much lower pressure than the permeate or the feed stream.
A compromise has to be found between high selectivity of the membrane, as the desire
is to extract O2 in greater proportions than N2, and permeability which dictates the
permeation rate, corresponding to the amount of molecules crossing the membrane per
unit of time or per unit of volumetric/mass of the feed stream.
The ideal membrane would present a high O2 selectivity and a high permeability, allowing
a high rate extraction of a highly O2 composed stream. However, these characteristics are
hardly coupled, which encourages the development of multi-stages configurations and the
development of different membranes to obtain both a high purity of the Nitrogen stream
and lower Nitrogen waste in the Permeate stream ([7]).
The membrane based ASU typically have production capacities in the range of 3 to 3000
Nm3/h of NES, corresponding to 0.75 to 90 tpd of NES. Depending on the combination
of membranes, the NES can commercially be obtained with a N2 molar purity between 90
and 99.9% ([7]). The NES pressure is usually in the range of 6 to 25 bar ([33]), but can
Page 8 of 150
==PAGE:15==
2
Air Separation technologies for Nitrogen production
also be atmospheric pressure, obtained from the simulations of one of the references used
in this study [7].
Page 9 of 150
==PAGE:16==
3
Base case techno-economic scenarios and TEA methods
3
Base case techno-economic scenarios and TEA meth-
ods
The common methods employed in the TEA for the three main current air separation
technologies as well as the techno-economic base case scenarios are presented in this
section.
In particular, the electricity supply options, electrolyzers technologies, economic assump-
tions and parameters, as well as the O2/H2 combustion and compression processes detailed
in this section will be conserved for the TEA of the electrochemcial O2 pump.
3.1
Air Separation Technologies: costs and performances extrac-
tion from Literature plants references
In order to compare the different air separation technologies, the most complete and recent
papers from the literature were selected for each technology.
In particular, to evaluate the costs and energy requirements regarding the production
of N2S, a list of parameters had to be extracted if provided, or fixed if missing. These
particular parameters are:
• The NES production rates (expressed in volumetric flows in Nm3/h, or in mass flows
in tpd or ton per year (tpy)).
• The molar composition of the produced NES (%mol of Nitrogen, Argon (Ar) or
Oxygen).
• The state of the products (liquid or gaseous), temperature and pressure of production
of the NES.
• The energy requirements (in kWh/kg or in kWh/Nm3).
• The Capital Expenditures of the plant.
• The Operational Expenditures (OPEX) of the plant.
• The resulting Levelized Cost of Nitrogen Enriched Stream.
• The cost of electricity used in the paper (in or $/MWh).
• The reference year and the money currency used in the paper for economic consider-
ations.
3.2
TEA scenario combinations comparison values
The TEA results are obtained using the plants characteristics found in the Literature and
the different base case scenarios, which result from the combinations of:
Page 10 of 150
==PAGE:17==
3
Base case techno-economic scenarios and TEA methods
• The year of investment (2020, 2030 or 2050),
• the electricity supply option,
• and the electrolyzer technology used for Hydrogen production.
For each literature reference, the different N2S productions are evaluated over a 30-year
period from the investment year, assuming that the production rates and the energy
requirements are steady over the whole period.
For each investment year, the optimal combination of the electricity supply option and the
electrolyzer technology (for the pure and pure & compressed N2 streams) are retained for
the final comparison of the different plants, yielding to the minimization of the following
values:
• the LCON regarding NES production,
• the LCON regarding pure N2S production,
• the LCON regarding pure & compressed N2S production,
• the energy needs regarding NES production,
• the energy regarding pure N2S production,
• the energy needs regarding pure & compressed N2S production.
The N2S LCON are simply calculated by summing the initial investment and the annual
operational costs and then dividing these global overall costs by the total mass of stream
produced over the 30-year period.
A more accurate way to calculate the levelized costs would have been to calculate the net
present values, by weighting the annual operational expenditures over the years with an
interest rate common to all the plants; but no time value of money was considered, as the
assessment of the different periods of income and expenses for every plant would have
considerably increased the work of extraction and data analysis from the literature.
It was considered that this approach would still allow a reliable comparison of
the different plants.
3.3
Reference year for currency, CEPCI, inflation rates and
currencies converting ratios
The reference year considered for economics is 2020 and monetary values have been con-
verted into US dollars value in 2020, represented with the following denotation ”$2020”.
Hence Chemical plant cost indexes (CEPCI) have been used to assess and compare the
capital costs of the different technologies studied in the same year of reference; as well
as inflation rates extracted from [4] and [1] for Euro Zone, and from [3],[2] for USD, and
currency converting ratios obtained with [23].
Page 11 of 150
==PAGE:18==
3
Base case techno-economic scenarios and TEA methods
3.4
Electricity supply and costs consideration
As the comparison of the different technologies was meant to be realized over the lifetimes of
the plants, predictions of electricity prices have been considered according to different case
scenarios of electricity supply of green fuel productions plants describe in the ”MarE-fuel:
LCOE and optimal electricity supply strategies for P2X plants” report [10]; part of the
marE-fuel project financed by the Maritime Fund and the Lauritzen Fund.
The assumptions and the different electricity supply strategies for the fuel production
plants are detailed in the report, and were conserved for this techno-economic analysis of
the NES Production Technologies.
In particular, three main plant electricity supply strategies were considered:
• Off-grid fuel plant, where the plant has its own source of renewable electricity which is
available ’Behind The Meter’, and hence implying a fuel production independent
of fluctuations in the electricity market;
• Grid connected fuel plant, where the electricity is bought directly from the Grid.
Two sub-cases were considered whether the plant production is inflexible and
electricity must be purchased from the grid independently of the market fluctuations,
or the production can vary to maximize the fuel production by acting as a grid
opportunist;
• Mixed fuel plant, where both previous cases are mixed (BTM-Grid mix) as the
fuel production plant buys electricity from the grid while having its own source of
renewable electricity.
The fuel productions are also assumed to have Energy Storage modules, and a distinction
is made between two cases for the electrolyzer technology (AEC or SOEC).
Also note that for the cases of inflexible production, the plants were ”constrained to
produce 430 kt of ammonia or 400 kt of methanol per year (same amount of energy)”. The
yearly production of 430 kt of ammonia correspond to a stoichiometric yearly production
of 353.65 kt of N2.
In the original study, Solar, On-shore and Off-shore wind renewable energy technologies
were assessed in four different locations with their specific energy resources and markets
(Esbjerg-Denmark, Arica-Chile, Ceduna-Australia, and Dakhla-Western Sahara).
For simplicity, only one set of results was retained in this study: the results obtained for
Esbjerg in Denmark, as Denmark owns one of the biggest shipping company in the world
with Maersk, which has particular interests in the green fuel transition for its fleet, either
with green methanol or green ammonia, which requires green N2 (and green H2).
The chosen investment years for the study are 2020, in order to compare the current
technologies and market situation with known parameters, but also in 2030 and 2050, as
the future targets and progress regarding the green electricity/energy sectors as well as
Page 12 of 150
==PAGE:19==
3
Base case techno-economic scenarios and TEA methods
the green H2 production have a major influence on the energy requirements and costs of
the different technologies.
The combination of the different electricity supply options detailed above led to the
consideration of 24 different electricity supply scenarios (8 for each investment year).
The electricity prices have been considered constant over 10-year sub-periods, based on the
values extracted from [10], and averaged over the 30-year period from the investment year.
The scenarios and resulting electricity prices, given in €2019, are represented on the Figure
1 and summarized in Table 1:

3.5
Hydrogen supply and costs considerations
The energy requirements and the costs of production of the Hydrogen required for the
final purification step by combustion of the remaining Oxygen in the NES (detailed in
the next subsection 3.6) have been calculated according to the different electricity supply
scenarios previously presented and the results from a techno-economic analysis of green
hydrogen production technologies [27].
For each of the investment years (2020, 2030 or 2050), the H2 production energy require-
ments are assumed to be constant, but the costs are varying with electricity prices over
the 30-year economic lifetime of the NES production plants. Even though the electrolyzers
were supposed to have an economic life of 25 years in the original paper, it was assumed
for simplicity that all the plants benefit from the same electrolyzers costs for the whole
30-year period of NES production.
This simplification was assumed not to have a great impact as the costs extracted from
the paper are the levelized costs of Hydrogen, to which electricity consumption costs are
readjusted with the electricity price resulting from one of the 24 base case scenarios.
Page 14 of 150
==PAGE:21==
3
Base case techno-economic scenarios and TEA methods
These electricity costs, initially expressed in 2019/MWh, result in H2 prices expressed in
2019/kg of H2, before being converted in $2019/kg of H2 and finally in $2020/kg of H2.
The assessed technologies of electrolyzers are listed below:
• Low-Temperature and Pressure Alkaline Electrolysis Cells (LTP-AEC), producing
hydrogen at 30 bar,
• Low-Pressure Solid Oxide Electrolysis Cells (LP-SOEC), producing hydrogen at 1
bar,
• Low-Pressure Solid Oxide Electrolysis Cells (LP-SOEC) + compressor, producing
hydrogen at 1 bar and compressed to 30 bar,
• Low-Pressure Solid Oxide Electrolysis Cells With Free Steam* (LP-SOEC WFS),
producing hydrogen at 1 bar,
• Low-Pressure Solid Oxide Electrolysis Cells With Free Steam* (LP-SOEC WFS) +
compressor, producing hydrogen at 1 bar and compressed to 30 bar,
• High-Temperature and Pressure Alkaline Electrolysis Cells (HTP-AEC), producing
hydrogen at 30 bar,
• High-Pressure Solid Oxide Electrolysis Cells (HP-SOEC), producing hydrogen at 30
bar,
• High-Pressure Solid Oxide Electrolysis Cells With Free Steam* (HP-SOEC WFS),
producing hydrogen at 30 bar.
The With Free STeam - ”WFS” electrolyzers (*) correspond to scenarios where some
heat is available from methanol or ammonia synthesis plant, and can be used ”freely” to
produced water steam and use it to produce H2, hence called WFS H2 ([27]).
In the present study, it was chosen to consider free steam availability only for H2 production
aiming at the purification by O2/H2 combustion of a NES followed by the compression
step, under the assumption that this pure & compressed N2S would ultimately be used to
synthesise NH3, which hence justify the existence of an ammonia production plant near
the NES production plant, and the induced heat availability.
No free steam was hence considered for the scenarios of pure N2 stream production, and
neither for NES production, which does not require H2.
It was considered that the ammonia synthesis can yield in the production of 1.5 ton of
high-pressure steam per tonne of NH3 produced ([27]).
Page 15 of 150
==PAGE:22==
3
Base case techno-economic scenarios and TEA methods
Using the hydrogen mass ratio of Hydrogen in water molecules of 0.112 (corresponding to
a ratio of 8.94 kg of H2O per contained kg of H2) and the small excess of steam necessary
in reality tpd produce hydrogen from steam, it was assumed, as often in the literature,
than 9 kg of steam (H2O) were necessary to produce 1 kg of H2.
Based on these figures, the production of 1 kg of NH3 allows the production of 0.1667 kg
of H2 WFS (1/9 of the NH3 mass production).
Considering the lowest NES intermediate purity used in the present paper of 90%mol of
N2, 0.016 kg of H2 must be produced for the combustion of the remaining 10%mol of O2
still present in the NES to obtain a pure N2 stream. It also corresponds to a mass of
0.0132 kg of H2 for the production of a sufficient stoichiometric mass of N2 for the further
synthesis of 1 kg of NH3.
As the free steam available from the production of 1 kg of ammonia would allow the
production of 0.1667 kg of H2 WFS, and the maximum H2 needs for the combustion of
the remaining O2 traces in an N2 enriched stream is only of 0.0132 kg for the future
production of 1 kg of ammonia, the WFS electrolysers energy needs from [27] could indeed
be considered for the production of a pure and compressed N2 stream, as it would be, in this
case, possible to use free steam provided by the excess heat of the associated ammonia plant.
Also note that most of the technologies presented produce hydrogen up to 30 bar, but LP-
SOEC technologies require a compressor to elevate the pressure of the produced hydrogen.
In particular, depending on the desired pressure of the pure N2S product, it might be
more interesting to use atmospheric pressure hydrogen or compressed hydrogen in order
to save potential compression energy requirements by reaching a higher pressure after the
combustion of the O2 impurities in the NES.
The specific energy consumption of the different electrolysers are summarized in the Table
2 in kWh/kg of H2 and in the Table 3 in kWh/Nm3 of H2, while the Hydrogen costs,
not including the electricity consumption cost, are summarized in the Table 4 in
$2020/kg.
The costs of Hydrogen can be calculated in $2020/kg using the costs Table 4 and adding
the electricity consumption cost obtained by multiplying the specific energy consumption
from the Table 2 and the electricity price of the chosen scenario in the Table 1.

Depending on the combination of the electrolyzer technology and electricity prices associ-
ated to one of the base case scenario, the electricity consumption can represent between
33 and 64% of the costs (in $2020/kg of H2) in 2020, between 45 and 69% of the costs in
Page 17 of 150
==PAGE:24==
3
Base case techno-economic scenarios and TEA methods
2030 and between 56 and 75% of the costs in 2050.
This highlights that with the progress made through the years for building cheaper and
more efficient electrolyzers, the electricity consumption will represent a great part of the
Hydrogen production costs, and that the electricity supply option and resulting price
of electricity is a major parameter for the production of hydrogen, and hence for pure
Nitrogen and ammonia productions too.
3.6
O2/H2 combustion as a final purification step
As the different technologies presented in the literature produce a NES with different
Nitrogen molar purity levels, a combustion of the remaining O2 traces with H2 is employed
to obtain an O2-purified N2S, allowing a comparison of the different technologies for a
product that only differs by the pressure, and that can further be compressed before a
potential NH3 synthesis†.
In the present paper, the O2/H2 combustion was considered under the following assump-
tions:
• The reaction of combustion between O2 and H2 was considered complete, adiabatic,
isochore, and in stoichiometric proportions: 1
2O2 + H2 →H2O, and considering the
ideal gas law.
• The initial gas mixture pressure was calculated based on the molar fractions and
respective initial pressure of the NES and H2 stream.
• The respective Low Heating Value and High Heating Value of Hydrogen considered
are LHV H2=120.1 MJ/kg of H2 and HHV H2=141.9 MJ/kg of H2.
The Low Heating Value of Hydrogen was used to control the maximum temperature
and pressure reached by the gas mixture, and the High Heating Value of Hydrogen
was used to evaluate the final pressure after a cool-down to ambient temperature
(20◦C).
• Liquid water was assumed to be removed from the combustion volume after cooling-
down and liquefaction, while the remaining pure N2 can occupy the whole initial
volume (hence leading to a slightly lower pressure).
• No reactor was considered for the reaction of combustion, meaning that the only
costs and energy requirements correspond to the production of the stoichiometric
H2 stream.
• For the few plants producing NES containing Argon, the energy requirements
and specific costs were adjusted afterwards, by reusing a corrected stream density.
†Some air separation plants or the electrochemical O2 pump products would still contain Argon.
Page 18 of 150
==PAGE:25==
3
Base case techno-economic scenarios and TEA methods
However, the heat capacity of Argon (around twice as low as N2 heat capacity in
J ·mol−1 ·K−1) was not considered, but is assumed not to noticeably change the final
pressure of the pure N2 stream obtained, due to the low initial molar concentration
of Argon in air or in the produced NES).
3.7
Compression of pure N2 gas
The production of pure & compressed N2 gas is considered by simply compressing the
pure N2 stream obtained after the complete purification by O2/H2 combustion of the
produced NES.
The methods presented in Morgan’s paper ([24]) were used: in particular the methods
detailed in the sections ”6.3.1 Compression power for the feed stream” for the calculations
of power requirements for the compression, ”6.3.6 Heat Exchangers” to calculate the flow
of cooling water required and ”6.3.7 Pumps” to calculate the power requirements for the
pumping of the cooling water.
The main assumptions and parameters for the compression, the cooling and the intercooling
water pumping processes are:
• An initial compression temperature of Tin = 20◦C (ambient temperature).
• An initial compression pressure Pin that varies according to the NES product and
resulting from the combustion of the remaining O2 traces.
• A final compression pressure of Pout = 150 bar, set to compare the plants for the
production of an identical product, potentially used for NH3 synthesis.
• A 5-step compression process (N = 5), requiring 4 inter-cooling steps (down to initial
and ambient temperature) to minimize the compression power requirements, with
an isentropic efficiency ηcomp = 0.75.
• A driver efficiency ηdriver = 0.95 for the electric motor of the compressors.
• Heat exchangers using water to cool the gas between the compression steps.
• An intercooling water temperature increase of 10◦C in the heat exchangers.
• A pumping efficiency of ηpump = 0.85 used to calculate the power requirements of
the heat exchangers intercooling water pumping.
• A gas polytropic exponent n=1.4017.
The main equation used for the calculation of the compression power requirements ˙Wcomp
is the equation 1 (reusing equation 53 of [24]):

This simplification is assumed not to imply noticeable deviation, as the Ar molar purity
would only be around 1.27% in a air stream from which all oxygen would be extracted, and
as N2 has a 0.297 kJ ·kg−1 ·K−1 specific gas constant, while Ar has a 0.208 kJ ·kg−1 ·K−1
specific gas constant.

The global energy requirements, including the compression and the pumping power of the
intercooling water, have been considered for the specific energy requirements of pure &
compressed N2 stream production, and were also included for the cost evaluation.
However, no costs of investment or maintenance were considered for the compressors, heat
exchangers or pumps for the pure & compressed N2 stream production economic analysis.
Page 21 of 150
==PAGE:28==
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using literature references
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using lit-
erature references
This section presents the different papers used as references for the TEA and comparison
of the current air separation and N2S production technologies, regarding the production
of NES, pure N2S and pure & 150 bar compressed N2S (for the synthesis of NH3).
The results based on the different studies found in the Literature are then treated using
the different methods and scenarios described in the previous section 3.
4.1
Cryogenic air distillations plants from literature:
Three papers describing cryogenic air distillation plants have been considered.
The first reference ”Techno-economic assessment of commercial ammonia synthesis methods
in coastal areas of Germany” ([29], by Shahmir Ali Nosherwani and Rui Costa Neto, referred
as Cryo 1) provides a comparison of natural gas conventional ammonia production and
green ammonia synthesis in coastal areas of Germany.
In particular, the stoichiometric Nitrogen production (in regards to the considered ammo-
nia production target) is detailed considering a Cryogenic distillation plant. The capital
expenditures of the plant, its operating costs and energy consumption, and provided.
The second reference ””Optimization of a novel cryogenic air separation process based on
cold energy recovery of LNG with exergoeconomic analysis”, ([36], by Y. Wu et al., referred
as Cryo 2) presents a novel three-column cryogenic air separation process integrated with
liquefied natural gas (LNG) regasification, optimizing waste Nitrogen utilization, cascade
heat transfer and multi-column air distillation to reduce the high energy requirements of
conventional cryogenic air separation.
The plant described in the paper produces Oxygen and Nitrogen with high purity levels,
both at liquid and gaseous states. The energy and economics assumptions, methods and
results are detailed, as well as the simulation results.
The third reference ”Detailed Design and Economic Evaluation of a Cryogenic Air Separa-
tion Unit with Recent Literature Solutions” ([37], by Andr´e F. Young et al., referred as
Cryo 3) details the techno-economic analysis of a modern cryogenic air separation plant
to be implemented in Brazil.
The paper provides hypothetical capital expenditures, manufacturing and operation and
maintenance costs, product streams and prices for sales of these products, as well as global
yearly energy consumption of the plant.
It is claimed in this paper that the plant mainly aims at oxygen production, but also consider
high-purity products (Nitrogen, Argon) with great precision, as well as the production
Page 22 of 150
==PAGE:29==
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using literature references
of ’cold’, ’heat’ and electricity, with great precision of the calculations, especially when
compared with other cryogenic plants studies in the Literature.
Note that this paper shows energy consumption much greater than the other references,
and that the plant is not considered as economically viable in the original paper, and
hence will be simply ignored for the overall comparison between the different technologies
and the electrochemical O2 pump.
4.1.1
1st Cryogenic ASU reference: Cryo 1
The main assumptions, characteristics and plant parameters reused from this paper ([29])
as inputs for the TEA are listed below:
• The reference year for monetary considerations is $2019.
• The 300 metric ton of ammonia produced per day, corresponding to stoichiometric
needs of 246.7 tpd of Nitrogen (produced as a liquid) and 53.3 tpd of Hydrogen.
• It is stated in the original paper that ”The high purity nitrogen (99%) is produced
from cryogenic air distillation at a rate 246.7 tons per day” and that ”Since HBP
requires high nitrogen purity, this paper will not cover the argon distillation”.
It was hence considered that the 1%mol of impurity was Argon and that all traces
of Oxygen were already removed from the Nitrogen stream, which implies than no
further O2/H2 combustion is required for the purification of the obtained N2 stream.
• The cryogenic Grass Root Cost is 24.51 mil. $2019.
• The total annual production costs without electricity are 6.77 mil. $2019.
• The plant yearly electricity needs are 25829 MWh/year.
To complete these parameters, the plant economic lifetime was assumed to be 30 years
(instead of the 25 years initially considered in the paper), with 8000 operating hours per
year, and without specific information, the produced NES produced was assumed to be
obtained at 1 bar of pressure at liquid state.
These input parameters and the detailed assumptions led to a final gaseous N2 stream
of 246.7 tpd N2, with a pressure at ambient temperature of 3.85 bar, and a N2 purity of
99%mol with 1%mol of Argon remaining.
The production of this NES requires 0.3141 kWh per kg, and comes at a cost of 0.0921
$2020/kg without electricity being included yet.
4.1.2
2nd Cryogenic ASU reference: Cryo 2
The main assumptions, characteristics and plant parameters reused from this paper ([36])
as inputs for TEA comparison are listed below:
Page 23 of 150
==PAGE:30==
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using literature references
• The production streams are:
– 15.63 tpd of gaseous N2 at a pressure of 11.76 bar at 20◦C (using ideal gas
law to estimate the pressure increase from 4.5 bar at −160◦C to 20◦C ambient
temperature), and with a N2 molar purity of 99.99%mol and 0.01%mol of O2
traces,
– 46.88 tpd of liquid N2 at a pressure of 4.5 bar at −180◦C, and a N2 molar
purity of 99.99%mol and 0.01%mol of O2 traces,
– 47.92 tpd of liquid O2 at a pressure of 1.5 bar and −179◦C, and a N2 molar
purity of 99.95%mol and 0.05%mol of O2 traces.
• A specific energy consumption of 0.258 kWh/kg of NES (for liquid, also considered
for gaseous).
• A specific energy consumption of 0.252 kWh/kg of Oxygen stream.
• 7300 hours of operation per year.
• Cost rate for Nitrogen streams of 5587.48 $/h (assumed to be $2019) for both the
liquid and the gaseous N2 streams.
The simulation model and parameters are presented in detail in the paper, as well as the
constants and assumptions for the economic analysis (cf Table 3 of the original paper [29]).
In particular, an electricity price of 71 $/MWh, and a 25-year process economic life were
chosen, as well as a 10% average general inflation rate. The production was however
considered over the 30-year period in the present study.
Using the specific energy consumption stated in the paper of 0.258 kWh/kg of liquid N2
and the used electricity price, it was possible to calculate the production costs without
including the electricity consumption yet, in order to include them again later in the TEA,
with the different scenario combination and associated costs of electricity.
The production cost of NES calculated from the paper parameters and assumptions was
6.515 $2019/ton of NES.
4.1.3
3rd Cryogenic ASU reference: Cryo 3
The main assumptions, characteristics and plant parameters reused from this paper ([37])
as inputs for TEA comparison are listed below:
• The reference year for monetary considerations is $2018. Also note that in this
paper, Brazilian Real was converted to USD with a R$ 4.00/US$ 1.00 ratio, and
costs updated using 2018 CECPI (PCI=603.1).
• The production streams are:
Page 24 of 150
==PAGE:31==
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using literature references
– 2143 tpd of gaseous Oxygen, with a selling price of 0.03019 $2018/kg, and the
associated sales benefits,
– 2100.1 tpd of gaseous Nitrogen, with a selling price of 0.02973 $2018/kg, and
with less than 5 ppm O2, and a pressure of 1.343 kgf per cm²,
– 385.74 tpd of liquid Oxygen, with a selling price of 0.30464 $2018/kg, and the
associated sales benefits,
– 214.3 tpd of liquid Nitrogen, with a selling price of 0.29569 $2018/kg, and with
less than 5 ppm O2, and a pressure of 1.347 kgf per cm²,
– 214.3 tpd of liquid Argon, with a selling price of 0.03019 $2018/kg, and the
associated sales benefits.
• The daily energy requirements are:
– Heat daily production: 471.46 MWh/day,
– Cold daily production: 1264.37 MWh/day,
– Electricity daily production: 1242.94 MWh/day, with a price of 15 $2018/MWh,
– For a global energy requirements of 2978.77 MWh/day.
• The total cryogenic plant capital expenditure are is 326.40mil. $2018, with a +- 35%
of accuracy.
• The total annual production costs without electricity are 52.165mil. $2018.
• 6333.78 operating hours per year.
To complete these parameters, the plant economic lifetime was assumed to be 30 years as
it was also considered for the other plants in this paper (even though a 20-year venture
period considered in the original paper).
The final NES obtained after mixing the gaseous and evaporated liquid product is a
gaseous stream flow of 2314.44 tpd of Nitrogen with a pressure of 1.66 bar at ambient
temperature (20◦C) and a molar purity of 99.9995%mol of O2 and 0.0005%mol of O2 traces.
The specific energy consumption is 1.21 kWh/kg of NES in theory, which is already by far
the highest figure among all the reference plants, but considering the whole plant electricity
needs, the number even rises up to 5.4 kWh/kg of NES which makes it completely out of
competition.
The levelized cost of NES (without electricity being included yet) is 0.0204 $2020/kg of
NES, but due to the very high energy consumption, the LCON ends up very high.
Unfortunately, this plant represented the only sample of very high capacity NES production
plant, but was not considered as relevant for the rest of the study.
Page 25 of 150
==PAGE:32==
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using literature references
4.2
PSA plants from literature:
Three sources have been considered to extract energy and economic data for the production
of NES by PSA technology plants.
The first source is the paper named ”Using hydrogen and ammonia for renewable energy
storage: A geographically comprehensive techno-economic study” ([30], by Palys and
al., referred as PSA 1), making the description of different chemical production plants
candidates for the production oh Hydrogen, Nitrogen and Ammonia.
In particular the Capex of the different plants is given for a reference capacity as well as a
scaling exponent (cf ref. Table 3), which was reused to estimate the Capex of other PSA
sources as a function of their production rate.
The Opex and the energy consumption are also provided in the paper (cf. Table 4 and 5
respectively).
The second source used to evaluate PSA economic and energetic performances is an
article from the supplier of compressed air and pneumatic equipment and solutions O’neil
company ([11], referred as PSA 2), about ”THE ENERGY COSTS ASSOCIATED WITH
NITROGEN GENERATOR”.
From the company’s article, it was possible to extract the energy requirements for different
N2 molar enrichment levels, but the capex and opex were not provided, and have been
estimated reusing the previous reference [31] for Capex, and another source ([28], by M.
Nordio and al.) to account for the global Opex.
The third source, ”High-purity nitrogen production from air by pressure swing adsorption
combined with SrFeO3 redox chemical looping” ([9], by B. Bulfin et al., referred as PSA 3),
presents the results of a simulation for NES production from a PSA unit coupled with a
downstream redox chemical looping to remove the remaining Oxygen in the pre-purified
stream.
This paper was considered as the energy requirements are claimed to be reduced compared
to other simple PSA ASU, but also because costs are expected to be reduced for the
production of high purity nitrogen at intermediate scale, by decreasing CAPEX and OPEX,
as the production capacity is also improved.
Although the paper does not include costs estimations, it was chosen to evaluate the costs
of this unit using the first source scaling factor. These costs would be an upper limit if the
coupling of the redox chemical looping indeed leads to lower energy consumption, CAPEX
and OPEX.
The results found for this paper were the most competitive, but had to be considered only
as potential performances, as no experimental proof of the technology was available, and
costs only assumed from another paper, even though is was claimed to the costs would be
reduced.
Page 26 of 150
==PAGE:33==
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using literature references
4.2.1
1st PSA ASU reference: PSA 1
The main assumptions, characteristics and plant parameters reused from this paper ([30])
as inputs for TEA comparison are listed below:
• A daily production of 2.467 tpd of gaseous N2 (which coupled to a hourly production
of 102.8 kg/h of N2 yield in a stoichiometric production of 125 kg/h of NH3),
• A 100% N2 molar purity considered for the NES, as the compression of the produced
NES was included in the original paper for the production of NH3, and indicating
than no O2 traces remain in the produced stream. Moreover, the production rates
are based on the stoichiometric proportions for the production of NH3, implying
that N2 and H2 streams were assumed to be pure.
• A 0.5 kWh/kg of 200 bar pure N2 compressed stream, to which the compression power
requirements from 1 atm to 200 bar was deduced to obtain the energy requirements
for a 1 bar NES.
• A 0.033 mil. $2019 CAPEX for a PSA reference plant with a production capacity
of 10 kg/h of N2 (or 240 tpd of N2), as well as a scaling exponent for PSA Capex
of 0.61 that can be found in the reference [31], by Palys et al., where the Table B.1
presents the Capital costs for a capacity range from 0 to 19.1 kg/h of N2 per hour
(or 0.4584 tpd of N2).
• An operational cost of 10 $2018/kg of N2.
The operating cost coefficient of the PSA plant provided in this paper are originally given
in [5] for the optimization of wind-powered ammonia generation.
The Opex retained is 10 $2018/kg of N2, and as no precise information were provided,
this Opex is assumed to be already including electricity consumption implying that no
electricity price variation can be applied for this particular plant from the Literature
concerning the production of NES.
However, as the NES is assumed to be pure, no electricity would have been consumed for
the production of H2 and the combustion of remaining O2 traces, and the compression costs
are calculated with varying electricity prices for the consideration of pure & compressed
N2 stream, so the impact of this fixed opex is limited to the initial NES production costs.
To assess the Capex of the 2.467 tpd of N2 PSA plant considered in the present paper,
it was assumed that the scaling exponent was still valid for a capacity five times greater
than the last capacity used for the scaling exponent calculations, after which the capital
costs are assumed to scale linearly, due to size and plant capacity limitations.
The factor of 5 was arbitrarily fixed to be in a typical range of PSA production rate,
without considering a range much greater than the original paper used to evaluate the
scaling exponent.
Page 27 of 150
==PAGE:34==
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using literature references
This method results in a last exponentially scaled Capex of 0.136 mil. $2020 for a capacity
of 95.5 kg of N2 per hour or 2.292 tpd of N2.
With a linear scaling until the PSA capacity of interest, a 0.1464 mil. $2020 is obtained
for a 102.8 kg of N2 per hour or 2.467 tpd of N2.
For a given production rate ( ˙mNES) within the range of exponential scaling, the CAPEX
can be calculated using the scaling exponent α0, the reference production rate ( ˙mNES, ref)
and the reference associated Capex CAPEX0 and the following equation 5:
CAPEX = CAPEX0 ·

˙mNES
˙mNES, ref
α0
(5)
If the production rate exceeds the scaling range, then a simple proportionality factor is
used from the last capacity value in the range of exponential scaling to the considered
production rate. It is equivalent as using the eq 5 with α0 = 1.
Finally, it was assumed that the plant counts 8000 hours of operation per year and that the
money currency used in the cited paper was $2019, as the paper was originally proposed
in December 2019.
4.2.2
2nd PSA ASU reference: PSA 2
The energy requirements and cost associated to the production of NES with 95%, 99.5%,
99.99% and 99.999% molar N2 purity levels were presented in the report from O’neil
company ([11], for a fixed production rate of 720 000 m3/year of NES, resulting (with 8000
hours of operation per year stated in the article) in a yearly production of approximately
2.7 tpd of NES, depending on the NES composition and density.
The production rates were reused to calculate the Capex of a PSA ASU reusing the
exponential equation 5 within the valid range, and the proportionality above the limit
production rate extracted from the previous reference [31].
An annual operation & maintenance percentage ratio of 5.3% of Capex (accounting for
the Bare Erected Costs, the container, transportation, assembly and contingency costs)
was considered, using a PSA Opex factor from a similar PSA technology used for H2
separation from natural gas grid, found in the Literature ([28], by M. Nordio and al.) to
account for the global operational costs.
The final retained figures for this PSA NES generators are summarized in the Table 5:

4.2.3
3rd PSA ASU reference: PSA 3
Only a few parameters could be extracted from ([9]) as inputs for TEA comparison:
• The energy consumption of 14 kJ/mol of N2 for a NES with a N2 molar purity
superior to 99.9997%mol of N2 (less than 3 ppm of O2 in the NES), corresponding to
a volumetric energy consumption of 0.1735 kWh/Nm3 of NES and a specific energy
consumption of 0.139 kWh/kg of NES.
• The pressure of 7 bar for the gaseous product.
As it was claimed in the original paper that the costs would be reduced with this technology,
the production costs have been considered equal to the costs obtained for the previous
source and the highest purity NES production (0.012133mil. $2020 for a 99.999%mol N2
purity) for a mass production rate of 2.69976 tpd of NES.
8000 hours of operation were considered per year over the 30-year period.
4.3
Membrane-based ASU from literature:
For the membrane-based ASU technologies, only one paper was taken as a reference:
”Polymeric membrane materials for nitrogen production from air: A process synthesis
study” ([7], by M. Bozorg et al., referred as MEMB).
This paper compiles energy and economic analysis of commercialized membrane-based ASU
technologies (either using Polysulfone (PSF) or Poly-phenylene-oxide (PPO) a polymeric air
separation membrane materials), as well as optimized system configurations hypothetically
obtained with the combination of different membranes and materials.
Page 29 of 150
==PAGE:36==
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using literature references
4.3.1
Membrane-based ASU reference: MEMB
In this paper ([7]), the minimum costs for different NES purity levels were assessed, first
for the two commercialized membrane materials, and then evaluated for the combination
of high performances membrane materials in multi-stages systems, not commercialized yet.
Optimization were first realized considering the same membrane for all the stages, and
then allowing the choice of a different membrane for each stage of the N2 enrichment system.
With the different membrane materials being available and having their specific properties
of selectivity and permeance, the goals were to determine whether it was more interesting
to improve selectivity or permeance for the reduction of NES production costs, as well as
to assess the combination of different membranes in a multi-staged unit to reach lower costs.
It appeared that the ”optimal membrane for all stages” scenario resulted in performances
similar to those obtained with ”an optimal membrane for each stage” configuration.
The authors noted that the permeance increase is more favorable but they also interestingly
noticed that the variable permeability offered by the second case (optimal membrane for
each stage) wasn’t improving the performances, and claimed that a moderate selectivity
had been found as the optimal choice.
The optimal energy requirements and levelized costs of the NES are summarized in the
Table 6 for the four levels of N2 molar purity: 90%, 95%, 99% and 99.9%.
It was considered in the present paper that the remaining impurities were O2
molecules, as no mention of Argon or any other gas is made in the original paper.
The economic approach is detailed in the paper, taking into consideration the CAPEX
(compressors, vacuum pumps and membrane modules), as well as OPEX including replace-
ment of membranes, energy consumption costs and maintenance.
An annual production rhythm of 8322 hours per year was considered in the original paper,
but no production rates were provided.
However, a figure adapted from [32] was reused in the paper, and describes an approximate
flow rate production range for membrane-based N2 enriched streams of 10 to 2000 Nm3/h
of NEA, which would correspond to a mass production rate range of 0.3 to 60 tpd of high
purity Nitrogen enriched stream (¿99% molar N2 purity).
A capacity of 10 tpd of N2 enriched stream has been chosen to represent the results of
these membrane-based technologies.
The optimal performances stated by this paper ([7]) (cf Table 5 in the original study) were
reused and are adapted in the Table 6.

price evolution with the selected scenarios. (The electricity price used by the authors was
44€/MWh, and it is assumed that costs were given in €2019).

4.4.2
Cryogenic distillation plants NES production energy requirements and

to decrease over the years, leading to lower LCON over the years. Hence, the different
plants are represented by 3 points, one for each investment year, on the LCON plot (right
hand side).

Figure 9: Membrane-based literature plants NES production energy requirements
(left) and LCON (right) as a function of the NES N2 molar purity
The tendency of the energy requirements and LCON increasing with the N2 molar purity
of the NES is really clear for the Membrane-based ASU, and seem almost linear until the
high purity levels, from which the increase is much more important.
Interestingly, one could notice that for low to medium N2 molar purity levels (from 90 to
95%mol N2), the energy requirements and the costs seem to be linked by a proportionality
factor, in the sense that lower energy needs would correspond to lower costs, and higher
energy needs to higher costs.
However, at high N2 molar purity levels (99 and 99.9%mol N2), the technologies seem
Page 42 of 150
==PAGE:49==
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using literature references
to offer either reduced energy needs but high costs, or high energy requirements but low
costs. At high purity, one would hence have to choose between the optimization of the
energy requirements or the optimization of the costs.
One should keep in mind that the energy requirements and costs are linked with the
electricity price, together with the fixed specific costs including investment and fixed
operation costs.
4.7
Current air separation technologies TEA global comparison
This section aims at comparing the three main ASU technologies to constitute the reference
market for the assessment of the competitiveness of the electrochemcial O2 pump, using
the selected representative and most competitive plants found in the literature for each
technology.
The results from all the different reference plants and their optimization (which were
displayed on the previous plots) have been summarized in Tables in the Appendix 9.
In particular, for each investment year, 6 tables have been created to present the optimal
scenarios and the corresponding results, regarding:
• the levelized cost of NES,
• the levelized cost of pure N2S,
• the levelized cost of pure & compressed N2S,
• the energy needs for NES,
• the energy needs for pure N2S,
• the energy needs for pure & compressed N2S.
One can also found in the Appendix 9 the energy requirements and LCON of all the NES
production extracted from the literature, as a function of the N2+Ar molar purity and
the year of investment (Figures 44, 45, 46 and 47).

It can be observed that the PSA NES production resulting from the simulation would
eventually present the lowest energy consumption for the production of pure and pure &
compressed N2S. However, the results are obtained from a simulation, and still have to be
Page 47 of 150
==PAGE:54==
4
TEA of the Air Separation Technologies using literature references
demonstrated.
The Cryogenic plants and two of the chosen PSA NES productions present the lowest
energy needs for the production of pure N2, whereas the Membrane-based technologies
present higher energy consumption that all the other technologies (except for the highest
purity NES production from PSA 2, 4.2.2).
Finally, it can also be noticed that the plants producing NES at elevated pressure (higher
than atmospheric pressure) benefit from a much lower increase of the energy requirements
when going to a compressed product.
It is due to the fact that the compression power requirements involve a pressure ratio
(cf eq. 1) and not on the absolute pressure difference between the initial and the final
pressure.
It means that a few more bars of pressure in the initial purified product can save a
lot of energy in the case of further compressed product, from which benefit mainly the
membrane-based and cryogenic NES productions.
The Figure ?? shows the LCON associated to the production of purified N2S or pure &
compressed N2S, depending on the investment year.
From an economic point of view, the hypothetical PSA plant PSA 3 (4.2.3), resulting from
a simulation without cost estimation in the original paper [9], as well as the Cryogenic
plant Cryo 2 (4.1.2) present the lowest LCON for both the production of pure and pure
& compressed N2S.
Then come the two PSA plants producing a 99.5%mol (N2+Ar) and a 99.99%mol (N2+Ar)
(PSA 2, 4.2.2), that are, as the cryogenic plants, slightly influenced by the expected
decrease in electricity prices and H2 specific energy consumption in 2030 and 2050, as
their energy consumption are pretty low already, and the purity of the produced rather
high, and hence not requiring a lot of H2 for the purification by combustion.
Note that in 2050, some of the Membrane-based process could actually out compete these
PSA plants, as their LCON is heavily composed by the energy consumption which are, as
seen in the Figure 14, higher than most of the other technologies, but also require more
H2 for the purification of the produced NES, as they present relatively lower purity levels.
Hence, the expected electricity prices decrease in 2050 would strongly reduce the LCON,
up to half of the LCON comparing 2050 and 2030 figures.
Note that a Figure (48) showing the energy requirements and the LCON associated to the
production of NES from the different chosen plant depending on the investment year is
provided in the Appendix 9.

==PAGE:60==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characteriza-
tion: methods and results
The electrochemical O2 pump presented in the present paper is a technology developed
in DTU Energy laboratories, reusing the basic principles of ion transport by applying
an electrical current to a cell composed by two electrodes separated by an electrolyte,
allowing the passage of ions but not of electrons, circulating in the external circuit.
As for Solid Oxide Electrolysis Cells (SOEC) producing Hydrogen (H2) and Oxygen (O2)
from water steam (H2O) by allowing the oxide ions to pass through the electrolyte to be
separated from the hydrogen atoms, the presented electrochemical technology allows the
extraction of a (great) part of the oxygen molecules from a N2/O2 gas mixture.
The performances of this electrochemical O2 pump have been tested for the
extraction of O2 molecules and the production of a NES from a N2/O2 feed
gas only, and not directly with air, which naturally contains 1%mol of Ar.
As Argon is an inert gas, such as Nitrogen, it is assumed that the performances of the
tested technology should not differ noticeably with the presence of 1%mol of Argon in the
feed gas. In theory, as both gas molecules should not have any reaction with the electrode
but only molecular/mechanical interactions, the molecules would only differ by their radius
(Nitrogen has an atomic radius rN = 65 pm, and Argon has an atomic radiusrAr = 71 pm,
note that N2 molecule has a different shape and lengths) and molar mass (28,0134 g/mol
for N2 and 39,948 g/mol for Ar), which should not have an influence on the extraction of
O2 molecules from the feed stream.
The 1%mol of Argon was replaced by 1%mol of N2 in the experiments (to keep a 21%mol
of O2 in the feed gas, as it is naturally the case in the air), which implies that to evaluate
the equivalent N2 molar purity that would be obtain from an air feed stream, the N2 molar
purity obtained in the experiments should be multiplied by a ratio of
78
78+1 to account for
the 1%mol of Argon that would initially be present in the air.
5.1
Technology principles and performance indicators
The fundamental design of the electrochemical cell as well as the characterization methods
and equations are detailed in this subsection.
5.1.1
Electrochemical O2 pump cell design and reaction
The electrochemical Oxygen pump technology is based on LSF/CGO electrochemical cells
prepared in DTU laboratories.§
§LSF stands for Lanthanum Strontium Iron (La(0.8)Sr(0.2)FeO(3)) and CGO for Cerium Gadolinium
Oxygen (Ce(0.9)Gd(0.1)O(1.95)).
Page 54 of 150
==PAGE:61==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
These cells allow the formation and the transport of oxide ions (O2−) from the cathode (air
electrode) to the anode through the porous electrolyte, following the Oxygen Reduction
Reaction (ORR, ) at the cathode: O2 + 4 e– −−→2 O2–, and the reverse reaction occurring
at the anode: 2 O2– −−→O2 + 4 e–.
The used materials confer to the electrolyte a great ionic conductivity as well as a very
low electronic conductivity, to the point that a 100% current efficiency can be considered;
meaning that the whole current provided to the cell is actually allowing oxide ions to be
formed and transported from one electrode to the other.
5.1.2
Oxygen extraction rate
With a 100% current efficiency, the extraction rate of Oxygen atoms from the feed gas
ψO2 extr (in mol/s) can be expressed by the following equation 6:
ψO2 extr =
−I
ne−· F (in mol/s),
(6)
where I is the current provided to the cell (in A), ne−is the number of electrons involved in
the electrochemcial reaction involving one molecule of O2 (ne−= 4), and F is the Faraday
constant (F = 96485.3 C · mol−1).
5.1.3
NES Nitrogen molar purity
The N2 molar purity (N2 mol %) of the produced NES corresponds to the molar fraction
xN2 NES simply expressed in %.
As the feed stream used in the experiments was simply a mix of N2 and O2 (whereas
air would contain 1%mol of Ar), the molar fractions of N2 xN2 NES and O2 xO2 NES in
the NES can be expressed using the O2 extraction rate ψO2 extr (in mol/s) as well as the
mixed feed streams of N2 ΦN2,feed (in kg/s) and O2 ΦO2,feed (in kg/s) with the following
equation 7:
xN2 NES =
ΦN2 feed/MN2
ΦN2,feed/MN2 + ΦO2 feed/MO2 −ψO2 extr
= N2 mol %
100
,
(7)
where MN2 is the N2 molar mass (28.0134 · 10−3 kg/mol) and MO2 is the O2 molar mass
(31.9988 · 10−3 kg/mol).
5.1.4
NES molar mass, normal density and flows
The molar mass of the electrochemical O2 pump produced NES MNES (in kg/mol) can
be calculated from the N2 the molar fraction xN2 NES, the N2 molar mass MN2 and the
Page 55 of 150
==PAGE:62==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
O2 molar mass MO2, using the following equation 8:
MNES = xN2,NES · MN2 + xO2,NES · MO2 (in kg/mol),
(8)
The NES normal density ρNES (in kg/m3) can then be calculated reusing the NES molar
mass MNES (in kg/mol), the gas constant R (8.314 J · mol−1 · K−1), as well as the normal
pressure and temperature Pnorm = 1.01325 bar and Tnorm = 0◦C and the equation 9:
ρNES = Pnorm · MNES
R · Tnorm
(in kg/m3)
(9)
The NES mass flow ΦNES (in kg/s) can be obtained using the Oxygen molar extraction
rate ψO2 extr (in mol/s) and the feed in streams of N2 ΦN2,feed (in kg/s) and O2 ΦO2,feed
(in kg/s), with the equation 10:
ΦNES = ΦN2,feed + ΦO2,feed −ψO2 extr · MO2 (in kg/s)
(10)
The NES volumetric flow φNES (in NL/h) can finally be obtained using the NES mass
flow ΦNES (in kg/s), the NES normal density ρNES (in kg/m3) and the equation 11:
φNES = ΦNES
ρNES
· 3600 · 103 (in NL/h)
(11)
5.1.5
Cell power and power density
For a given cell active surface area (A in cm), a current or a current density (I in A or
i = I/A in A/cm), as well as a cell voltage (V in V ), the cell power density (Pdens in
W/cm) and cell power (P in W) are obtained with the equation 12:
Pdens = V · i (in W/cm2), or
P = Pdens · A = V · i · A = V · I (in W).
(12)
5.1.6
Specific and volumetric NES energy consumption
From the power P (in W) and the NES mass flow ΦNES (in kg/s) or the NES volumetric flow
φNES (in NL/h), the specific energy consumption εNES (in kWh/kg) and the volumetric
energy consumption ζNES (in kWh/Nm3) can be calculated with the following equations
13:
εNES =
P
ΦNES
·
1
3600 · 103 (in kWh/kg),
ζNES =
P
φNES
(in kWh/Nm3)

Page 56 of 150
==PAGE:63==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
5.1.7
Area Specific Resistance and Joule losses
The Area Specific Resistance (ASR, in Ω· cm2) represents the cell resistance during
operation and corresponds to the combination of the ohmic resistance of the cell components
(electrodes and electrolyte) as well as the polarization resistance resulting from the
application of an external potential, that can further be decomposed into:
• activation polarization, associated to the charges transfer at the electrode/electrolyte
interface and kinetics reaction rates differences,
• ohmic polarization, associated to electrical resistances of the cell components,
• concentration polarization, associated to the gradients of concentration of the species
at electrode levels.
The ASR can be assessed from impedance spectroscopy and then can be used to evaluate
the Joule losses, resulting in heat production induced by the passage of the electric current
through the cell.
These Joule losses (in W) can be assessed using the ASR (in Ω· cm2), the current I (in
A), and the current density i (in A/cm2) by adapting the Joule’s first law (eq 14, in W):
˙QJoule = ASR · I · i (in W),
(14)
5.1.8
Utilization rate
The utilization rate UR (in %) is defined as the ratio between the Oxygen atoms extracted
from the feed stream and the Oxygen atoms initially present in the feed stream. It is
in other words an ”extraction rate efficiency” defined in %, according to the following
equation 15:
UR = ψO2 extr · MO2
ΦO2,feed
= ΦO2,extr
ΦO2,feed
(in %),
(15)
where ψO2 extr is the molar O2 extraction rate (in mol/s), ΦO2,extr is the mass O2 extraction
rate (in kg/s) and ΦO2,feed is the O2 feed in stream (in kg/s).
Note that the utilization rate UR (in %) is directly linked to the NES N2 molar purity
N2 mol % (in %) by the following equations 16:
N2 mol % =
ΦN2,feed/MN2
