Page 57 of 150
==PAGE:64==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
5.2
Performed experimental tests
The cell characteristics, as well as the experimental setup and performed experiments are
detailed below.
5.2.1
Cell design and manufacturing
The LSF/CGO cells manufactured in DTU Laboratories are square shape cells with an
active surface area of 16 cm2, two symmetric Pr6O11 infiltrated LSF/CGO electrodes (3
of 4 cycles of infiltration are realized) with a 500 µm thickness, and a CGO electrolyte
with a 500 µm thickness.
The cells are produced by a Tape Casting process, followed by a screen printing test to
delimit the active area and ensure the sealing of the cell and the current collector on the
ceramic basement.
5.2.2
Experimental setup and main parameters
During the experiments performed to evaluate the electrochemical process and cell per-
formances, the tested sample (the cell) is placed on a ceramic basement used to stabilize
the experiment environment but also to conduct the feed gas from inlet to outlet. The
ceramic material also offer an electrical insulation, and can support high temperatures.
The cell and the ceramic basement are then placed in a electrical furnace, controlled by
thermocouples placed close to the cell center and in the corners of the furnace. The cell
potential is also measured between the two electrode sides, and the external circuit is
connected to the power supply.
The objective of the electrochemical O2 pumping process is to extract Oxygen atoms from
air streams, however, to ensure a better accuracy of the feed gas flow, and avoid the costs
of using pure Argon to be mixed, is was chosen not to use directly air, but to use a mix
of a pure N2 stream and a pure O2 stream, with respective molar fractions of 0.79 and
0.21. This way, the feed gas flow could be controlled precisely, the Oxygen concentration
is similar to O2 concentration in air, and costs of mixing pure Argon to recreate air are
saved.
Due to Argon being an inert gas, it is assumed that the process would present the same
performances with 1%mol of N2 being replaced by 1%mol of Ar.
However, considering an experiment where:
• the initial feed stream N2 molar purity is [N2]feed,exp,
• the initial feed stream O2 molar purity is [O2]feed,exp,
• the [Ar]air = 1 mol% of Argon usually present in the air was replaced by N2 in the
feed stream,
Page 58 of 150
==PAGE:65==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
• the produced NES has a final N2 molar purity [N2]NES,exp,
• the produced NES has a final O2 molar purity [O2]NES,exp,
one could calculate the expected corrected N2 molar purity in the NES [N2]NES,corr, using
the equation 17:
[N2]NES,corr = [N2]feed,exp −[Ar]feed,corr
[N2]feed,exp
· [N2]NES,exp
(17)
On the other cell side, some gas if blown to take away the molecules extracted from the
feed stream from the feed side through the electrolyte.
In the performed experiments, the sweep gas used is either air or pure O2, delivered to the
other side of the cell with great flows to flush the extracted gas ant to maintain a constant
O2 partial pressure at this side of the cell (which is possible if the O2 extracted flow is
much lower than the sweep gas flow). Note that when pure O2 is used, even though a
great flow wouldn’t change the composition as Oxygen is mixed with the Oxygen stream,
the same great flows are used, which allow the comparison of the two sweep gas influence
on the results under the same conditions.
To characterize the cells and its performances, different experimental tests were performed,
and are detailed in the next section.
The main parameters for the experiments are:
• The temperature at the center of the cell, between 500◦C and 800◦C.
• The current, between 0 and -16 A, or the corresponding current density between
0 and -1 A/cm2 considering a cell active area of 16 cm2 (the current is counted
negatively as it is provided to the cell and not produced by the cell).
• The feed gas flow (in NL/h) and composition, resulting from the molar mix of 79%
pure N2 and 21% of pure O2, to keep molar proportion of Oxygen the same as in
the air, but with replacing the 1% mol of Argon by Nitrogen, for a more precise flow
controlled by mass flow controllers, but still the cost of using pure Argon to simulate
air feeding.
• The sweep gas flow (in NL/h), set at high flow rates (50 NL/h) to flush the O2
extracted from the feed gas and maintain a constant O2 partial pressure and the
sweep gas composition, either pure O2 stream or air.
• The pressure of the feed gas and sweep gas, which is simply normal pressure.
Note that a current or current density increased would imply an increase of the cell
potential and of the power, as well as a higher O2 extraction rate and hence higher purity
levels for fixed feed gas flows, but would also intensify the cell degradation.
Page 59 of 150
==PAGE:66==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results

the same current density range but differ by their feed gas flows, implying a ’low’ or a
’high’ utilization rate.

The 14 experiments referred as the Low UR tests have been realized with a total feed gas
volumetric flow of 26.6 NL/h, with a molar composition of 79% of N2 and 21% of O2, for
a ’maximum’ current of -16 A (except for the test 14, where the maximum current density
was -14.2 A).
Using the equation 15, the maximum utilization rate of these experiment is 59.61%, and is
referred as the ”low utilization rate” in the present study.
During each of these 14 experiments, the current has been progressively pushed from 0 to
-16 A (or -14.2 A for the test 14), with 1 −A step, with stabilization duration of 20 to 90
seconds.
The tests were performed from 800◦C to 500◦C with a 50◦C step, which results in 7
different temperature set-points. For each of these temperature set-points, a first test was
realized with pure O2 as sweep gas, and a second test was realized with air instead.

values are greater when air is employed as sweep gas instead of pure O2, with an increase
of between 20% and 40%.

The first seven tests as well as the tests 13 and 14 are performed with the same current
and feed gas streams as the low UR tests, and hence are not of primary interest here.
However, unlike the low UR tests and the nine similar tests mentioned just above, the five
tests from 8 to 12 were all performed at a temperature close to 700◦C, with lower feed gas
volumetric flows (still with a gas molar composition of around 79% of N2 and 21% of O2),
but with the same maximum current value (-16 A), which implies higher utilization rates.
The maximum utilization rates (calculated using the equation 15) of these five different
high UR tests are: 79.48% for the test 8, 84.72% for the test 9, 89.73% for the test 10,
94.84% for the test 11, and 98.77% for the test 12.
Note that Air was used as the sweep gas for all of these high UR tests, and
hence unfortunately does not allow the comparison with tests using pure O2
as sweep gas at high UR.
The current has also been increased progressively from 0 to -16 A, with 1 −A step and
with stabilization duration between 20 and 90 seconds.
Page 61 of 150
==PAGE:68==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
5.2.4
Other experimental tests
Impedance spectroscopy tests were also performed under operation, allowing the calcu-
lations of ASR values under operation, and some durability and reliability tests were
performed with the cells, and demonstrated great durability and very low voltage increase
over thousands of hours of operation for the different conditions of test.
Another test was performed to reach a 100% molar purity of N2 in the produced NES,
implying a total extraction of the O2 molecules in the feed stream, but the tested cell
finally broke before reaching the total purity. It was concluded that the cell broke because
of the mechanical stressed imposed by the O2 partial pressure difference between the feed
gas side and the sweep gas side.
A further analysis of the impedance graphs, durability and extreme extraction experiments
has not been done in this study, but would probably help to better characterize or operate
the cells, or even modify their design.
5.3
O2 pump performances at Low Utilization rates
The O2 pump performances resulting from the tests at Low UR are presented in this
section. Note that for a better comparison of the sweep gas options, the best results
obtained with pure O2 or air as sweep gas are later compared in the section 5.5.
5.3.1
Low UR - iV curves and PV curves
The next two Figures present the iV and PV curves (respectively representing the voltage
as a function of the current density or the power density as a function of the current
density), either with Utilization Rate as the secondary x-axis for Figure 15 or with N2
molar purity as the secondary x-axis for Figure 16).
On the left hand side, the figures show the results for the tests performed with Pure O2
as sweep gas, whereas the right hand side figures show the results obtained with Air as
sweep gas.
A first noticeable point is the temperature influence on the cell voltages: increasing the
cell center temperature from 500◦C by 50◦C steps indeed induces a decrease of the acti-
vation energy, as more power is provided as heat, and hence lowers the electrical power
requirements when the voltage difference is fixed, or, for a fixed current density (as it is
the case in the experiments of this study), lower the cell voltages, as the ASR values are
also lowered (cf Table 10).
Page 62 of 150
==PAGE:69==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
However, above 750◦C, it seems that the cell voltages couldn’t be lowered by an increase of
temperature, which means that the cell faces limitation phenomena (kinetic limitations of
the O2 extraction on the feed side, reach of an ASR minimum value) that supplementary
tests should help to identify.
One could also observe that the inflection of the iV curves tends to invert at low or high
temperatures: at 500◦C for instance, the slope is decreasing with an increase of the current
(in absolute terms), while the slope is increasing with the increase of the current at 750◦C
or 800◦C, which would imply that the cell voltages would increase at a higher rate with an
increase of the current density at high than at low temperatures, even though the voltages
are eventually higher at low temperatures.
In other terms, it seems that at low temperatures, increasing the current density comes
at lower and lower ”voltage costs”, whereas at high temperatures, increasing the current
density by a given value will induce higher and higher cell voltages raises.
It might be due to polarization phenomena (activation, ohmic and concentration polariza-
tion) evolution with current density at low or high temperatures.
Now, comparing the results obtained with the two sweep gases, it can be noticed that the
tests performed with pure O2 have an non zero OCV (as opposed to air sweep gas test)
due to O2 partial pressure between the feed gas and the sweep gas sides, described later
with Nernst potentials in this report (5.5.2).
Moreover, one can see that at lower temperatures (from 500◦C to 650◦C), the cell voltages
obtained with pure O2 as sweep gas are lower than the cell voltages obtained with air as
sweep gas, implying lower power consumption.
However, when the temperature overpasses 650◦C, air sweep gas tests present lower cell
voltages and become more energy efficient.
The best results obtained with pure O2 or air as sweep gas are later compared in the
section 5.5.

purity levels.

5.4.2
High UR - NES and pure N2 specific energy consumption
The Figure 20 shows the specific energy consumption of the O2 pump as a function of
Utilization Rate (with N2 molar purity as a secondary x-axis), with the tests done with
pure O2 as sweep gas on the left hand side and with air as sweep gas on the right hand
side.
One can observe that for the tests performed at high UR (from 8 to 12), the specific energy
consumption vary slightly, but let appear than the tests 11 & 12 are the tests showing the
best performances and the lowest energy needs.
The Figure 21 shows the NES specific energy consumption (in kWh/kg of NES) as a func-
tion of the N2 molar with the new High UR data set, where one could observe the further
purity levels and lowered energy needs with a delayed H2 utilization for the combustion of
the remaining O2 traces.
The Figure 22 displays the pure N2 stream specific energy consumption (in kWh/kg of
pure N2) as a function of the intermediate N2 molar purity reached by the O2 pump,
followed by a combustion of the remaining O2 traces with H2 to reach a 100%mol N2
purity.
One can notice that on the left hand side, the tests are performed with pure O2 as
sweep gas, without reaching a high N2 molar purity level, and show very similar energy
consumption curves, whereas three different operating temperature were tested, which
again indicates a limitation phenomena inducing that the temperature increase does not
reduce the energy requirements anymore at some point.
On the right hand side, for the tests performed with air as sweep gas, the temperatures of
operation are almost all around 700◦C (except for test 2 & 4).
The test 11 & 12 have been performed with high enough utilization rates (and reach the
corresponding N2 molar purity levels) to show a minimum in the energy needs for the
production of pure N2 stream.
These minimums actually show that an optimal intermediate N2 molar purity of the NES
exists and that this intermediate N2 molar purity level should be reached by the O2
pump before the final H2/O2 combustion. (Note that a specific energy consumption of
42 kWh/kg of H2 was again considered here).
In other terms, it means that even though the O2 pump can be used to reach a maximum
N2 molar purity of NES, to produce a pure N2 stream, one should either operate the O2
pump at a lower current density for the same feed gas flow, or increase the feed gas flow
and keep the current density, to reach the optimal Utilization Rate.

5.5
Analysis of the best experimental results at low UR (iV
curves, energy cons., reliability, durability...)
From the two data set presented in the previous subsection (5.2), the most interesting
results were selected and are further analyzed in this subsection.
The tests 3 & 4 of the Low UR data set were selected, as they presented the best results
and allow the comparison of performances for the two different sweep gas.
The tests 11 and 12 were selected in the High UR data, as they were showing the highest
purity, bust also an optimal for pure N2 production.
5.5.1
Best Low UR tests - iV and iP curves comparison - Pure O2 vs Air
sweep gas
The next figure 23 allows the comparison of the best Low UR Pure O2 (test 3) and Air
sweep gas (test 4) experiments with the iV curves on left hand side and the iP curves on
the right hand side.
One could see on the iV curves plots that the test performed with pure O2 shows higher
cell voltages than the test performed with air as sweep gas, at any fixed current density.
This results in higher power density, as observed on the right hand side graph.
Page 72 of 150
==PAGE:79==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
One could notice that the curve representing the test 3 (in green, pure O2 sweep gas)
displays, for a current density of 0 A/cm2, an OCV non equal to zero, due to the O2
partial pressure difference between the feed gas and the sweep gas, which causes the cell
potentials to be higher in the case of pure O2 sweep gas.
However, the slope displayed by the test pure O2 sweep gas test is smaller than the slope
of the air sweep gas test, which may lead to the hypothesis that from a given applied
current density, the green curve and hence the pure O2 test will cross the pink curve and
hence the air test, making the pure O2 sweep gas a more energy efficient process.
But applying a higher current density induces a higher degradation of the cells and its
materials, which may lead to the choice of limited current density levels, as well as the
utilization of air rather than pure O2 if the limit current density (regarding cell degra-
dation) is too low and still results in higher cell voltages when pure O2 is used as sweep gas.

5.5.2
Nernst potentials
The Nernst potential corresponds to the theoretical potential across a cell membrane that
would exactly oppose to the diffusion of a particular ion through the membrane. The
Nernst potential is defined by the Nernst equation 18:
Ecell = E◦
cell −RT
nF · ln Qr, (in V ),
(18)
Page 73 of 150
==PAGE:80==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
where Ecell is the actual cell potential (in V ), E◦
cell is the standard cell potential (in V ), R
is the gas constant (8.314 J · mol−1 · K−1), T is the temperature (in K), n is the number
of electrons transferred in the cell reaction or half-reaction, F is the Faraday constant
(96485.3 C · mol−1), and Qr (unit-less) is the reaction quotient, equal to the ratios of the
products and reagents activities.
In this study, the redox reaction occurring results in O2 Oxygen gaseous molecules
extraction, implying the formation of oxide ions O2−. The reaction quotient here hence
implies the Oxygen O2 molecules from the feed gas, and the Oxygen O2 molecules from
the sweep gas. Their respective activities can be assimilated to their concentration, or
even to their partial pressure as all the components are gaseous.
The Nernst potentials can then be expressed by the following equation 19:
Nernst Potential = RT
nF · ln PO2, sweep
PO2, feed (V )
(19)
where the number of electrons involved in the electrochemical reaction n = 4, and where
PO2, sweep and PO2, feed are respectively the O2 partial pressure in the sweep gas and in
the N2 enriched stream, which also correspond to the respective O2 molar fractions.
The next Figure 24 presents the Nernst potential as a function of the O2 molar composition
of the feed stream against either pure O2 sweep gas (on the left hand side) with an O2
molar composition of 100%mol, or air sweep gas (on the right hand side) with an O2 molar
composition of 21%mol.

Figure 25: Nernst Potentials gaps between Air and Pure O2 as sweep gases comparison

One could observe on this Figure 25 that for a fixed temperature, the difference in the
Nernst potentials between the two sweep gas cases is constant for all gas O2 molar
composition.
It is also interesting to note that these constant gaps between pure O2 and air sweep
gas Nernst potentials are lower at reduced temperatures (due to the proportional factor
including temperature in the Nernst potential factor).
It means that with a decreasing temperature, the over-voltage observed in the Nernst
potential when choosing pure O2 as sweep gas instead of air is also decreasing.
Page 75 of 150
==PAGE:82==
5
Electrochemical O2 Pump technology characterization: methods and results
In theory and regarding the Nernst potentials, by lowering temperatures it is hence still
but less and less interesting to choose air rather than pure O2.
Indeed, as the Nernst potential are only theoretical indicators and as some factors such
as ionic diffusivity or electrodes activity have a major influence during operation, it was
observed earlier that at low temperatures, pure O2 sweep gas tests were actually showing
lower cell voltages when compared to air sweep gas tests (cf Figure 15), which show lower
cell voltages when only Nernst potential are considered.
It is also worth to remind that under the same testing conditions, the ASR values are also
lower when pure O2 is used than when air is used as sweep gas (cf 9).
In the annex, the Figure 52 shows the specific energy requirements of the production of
NES as a function of the obtained N2 molar purity and the specific energy requirements of
the production of pure N2 stream as a function of the intermediate NES N2 molar purity
produced by the electrochemical O2 pump, for the tests 3 & 4 of the Low UR data set.

Page 78 of 150
==PAGE:85==
6
TEA of the O2 pump
6
TEA of the O2 pump
The techno-economic analysis of the electrochemical O2 pump is based on the performances
obtained with the experimental test performed at a high utilization rate, at 700◦C and
with air as sweep gas, as this test provided the highest range for the N2 molar purity, as
well as the lowest energy consumption (at relative purity levels).
The electrochemical O2 Pump experimental results reused in this section
correspond to the separation of N2 and O2 only. The separation of N2 and
O2 in air containing 1%mol of Argon would in theory not impact directly the
performances for the O2 extraction, but result in lower N2 purity as Argon
would still be present in the NES, and would require another process that has
not been studied in this paper, for the separation of N2 and Argon, if the goal
was the production of totally pure N2 stream.
Also note that:
• The same scenarios and possibilities for the electricity supply option and electrolyzer
were considered, and that the N2 streams productions are evaluated over a 30-year
period after investment in 2020, 2030, or 2050, with a steady production and 8000
hours of operation per year.
• The methods used for the main Air Separation Technologies (cf 3) have been reused
for the evaluation of the NES, pure N2 stream, or pure & compressed N2 stream
production by the electrochemical O2 pumping technology.
• The costs of the electrochemical O2 pump technology are evaluated for a wide range
of production capacities, as a function of NH3 yearly production and corresponding
stoichiometric N2 annual market penetration; the CAPEX is calculated with an
approach similar to that of SOEC technology CAPEX calculations.
• The OPEX are assessed using the electricity consumption driving the electrochemical
process, as well as the side production costs, the maintenance, insurance and labor
costs.
• The electrochemical cells used for the experiments are assumed to maintain the exact
same performances when placed in stacks.
The CAPEX and OPEX calculations of the electrochemical O2 pump are respectively
detailed in the subsections 6.1 and 6.2.
6.1
Electrochemical O2 pump Capex estimation principle
The SOEC and the LSF/CGO cells used by the presented electrochemical O2 pump
technology to produce NES share great similarities regarding the designs, materials, goals
Page 79 of 150
==PAGE:86==
6
TEA of the O2 pump
and physical principles (the transfer of oxide ions induced by the electrical power running
in the external circuit from one electrode side to the other through the electrolyte).
It was hence considered relevant to base the capital costs estimations of a Nitrogen
production plant using the electrochemical pump developed in DTU Energy on SOEC
CAPEX calculations approach, and in particular to use the methods described in ”Predict-
ing the price of solid oxide electrolysers (SOECs)”, published in 2016 by M´yrdal and al. [26].
The Capex estimation is based on the total surface of cells to be manufactured in one year,
also called production volume (in m2/year), which is grouping the cell surface requirements
of all the similar plants to be built to reach a given production capacity of H2 in the
original paper, or N2S in the present study.
This global production volume yields in a cell specific cost of manufacturing (in $/kW or
$/m2), which tends to decrease as the production volume increases, and which is hence
fixed for all the plants.
Based on the production volume, the stack costs can be evaluated, as well as the total
equipment costs of the plant.
The total energy consumption (MWh) necessary for a given NES production (ton per
year or ton over the lifetime) and the surface of cells required (in m2) are linked by the
specific energy consumption (kWh/kg of NES), the cell/stack power density (W/cm²)
and the production time (one year or lifetime period).
Eventually, factors are considered to account for the assembly, the profit, the grid con-
nection cost, as well as the land, buildings, instrumentation, engineering, contingency,
construction, etc, and finally obtain the total plant investment costs (in $/MW or in mil.$).
6.1.1
Electrochemical O2 Pump market penetration
In order to adapt the SOEC CAPEX calculations methods ([26]), a global NES yearly
production rate, corresponding to the cumulative production capacity of all the plants using
the electrochemical pumping process expected to be built. Using the energy consumption
and the power density of the cells, the cumulative power as well as the cumulative cell
surface requirements can also be obtained.
The ammonia production was estimated to 150 million metric tons in 2022 ([34]), which
would correspond to a stoichiometric N2 yearly production of 123.37 million metric tons
of N2.
A low and a high market penetration factors were respectively fixed to 0.0001% and
2% of the given stoichiometric N2 for NH3 market, meaning that every year, new NES
production plants using the electrochemical O2 pump technology would be built and that
Page 80 of 150
==PAGE:87==
6
TEA of the O2 pump
their gathered NES production would represent a daily production from 0.37 to 7402 tpd
of N2 contained in the produced NES.
Considering that the electricity supply scenarios were developed, in the original paper [10],
for green fuel production plants with a capacity of 430 000 ton per year of Ammonia, one
could consider that the corresponding stoichiometric N2 production plant would have a
capacity of 430000 · (MN2/2)
MNH3 = 354000/ tpy of pure contained N2, or a daily production
capacity of 1061 tpd of pure contained N2.
The low penetration case would correspond to the building of a small N2 production plant
with a capacity of 0.37 tpd of contained N2, and the high penetration case to the building
of 6 high capacity N2 production plants, with a production capacity 1057 tpd of contained
N2 each.
Hence, the evaluation of the electrochemical O2 pumping technology has been
realized with global capacities between 0.37 and 7402 tpd of contained N2.
6.1.2
Yearly NES production requirements
From a given yearly pure N2 production target and assuming 8000 hours of operation per
year, one can obtain the daily pure N2 production target Φpure N2 (in tpd of pure N2),
and for a given NES N2 mass fraction yN2 NES, the NES daily production rate ΦNES (in
tpd of NES) can be calculated using the following equation 21:
ΦNES = Φpure N2
yN2 NES
(in tpd)
(20)
The corresponding NES O2 mass fraction is yO2 NES = 1 −yN2 NES, as Argon is not
present in the feed stream.
The N2 mass fraction yN2 NES can be calculated using the respective molar fractions of
N2 (xN2 NES) and O2 (xO2 NES) in the NES (7 and the following equation 21:
yN2 NES =
xN2 NES · MN2
xN2 NES · MN2 + (1 −xN2 NES) · MO2
(21)
where MN2 and MO2 are the respective molar masses of N2 and O2 (in kg/mol).
6.1.3

consumption εNES (in kWh/kg of NES, obtained using the equation 13), and on the power
density Pdens (in W/cm2), the cell production volume CellProd.V olume (in m2/year) can be
expressed using the following equation 22:

was adjusted, if necessary, to a maximum power corresponding to the maximum NH3
production capacity described in [10].
6.1.5
Turnkey prices calculations
As employed in [26], once the initial investment for the stacks and the total equipment
costs are summed into an global initial capex (in $2016/kW), a factor α = 0.5 is used as a
mark-up to account for assembly, profit etc, and obtain an ’ab factory cost’ ab factory cost
(in $/kW) (eq 25:
ab factory cost = global initial capex · (1 + α) (in $2016/kW)
(25)
Then a grid connection cost β, originally fixed at 0.05 k€2016/kW ([26]) and readjusted
to β = 0.56 k$2016/kW (with a 1.12 €2016 to $2016 conversion factor) has been added,
except for ”Behind The Meter” electricity supply scenarios, as no connection to the grid is
necessary.
A final turnkey mark-up factor γ = 0.6 ([26]), accounting for land, buildings, instrumenta-
tion, engineering, contingency, construction etc ([26]) is then used to go from ab factory
cost to turnkey price (in $/kW), as described by the following equation 26:
Turnkey price = β · 1000 + (1 + γ) · ab factory cost (in $2016/kW)
(26)
The turnkey price can then finally be multiplied by the power to obtain the total investment
cost (in k$2016), to which the stack replacements costs can be added to obtain the total
investment costs over the whole economic lifetime, finally converted in k$2020.
The Table 19 summarizes the turnkey prices and the overall costs corresponding to the
costs of building the different plants to sustain the given N2S production:

6.2
Operating costs calculations methods and assumptions
The operational costs of the O2 pump have been estimated by taking into account:
• the electricity power directly used for the electrochemical reaction, by using the
average electricity price over the 30-year period of NES production and the specific
energy consumption of NES, pure N2 stream or pure & compressed N2 stream,
• the side production costs which include the maintenance, insurance and labor cost.
6.2.1
Electricity consumption costs
The electricity consumption costs were obtained by multiplying the total energy consump-
tion over the 30-year period of steady production by the calculated average electricity
price (depending on the scenarios combinations), and finally by dividing this product
by the total mass of the specific N2 stream produced over the 30-year period, resulting
in electricity costs in $2020/kg of NES, or kg of pure N2 or even kg of pure & compressed N2.
Page 86 of 150
==PAGE:93==
6
TEA of the O2 pump
For the production of pure N2S, as for the conventional N2 production technologies
considered in the present study (cf 3), only the production of the H2 necessary to the
O2 remaining traces combustion was taken into consideration from an economic and an
energy point of view. No investment costs or side energy consumption were considered for
the combustion system or reactor.
For the production of pure & compressed N2 stream, again, as for the conventional N2
production technologies considered in the present study (3), the methods used can be
found in [24].
Both for the pure N2 stream and the pure & compressed N2 stream production by the
electrochemical O2 pump, the considered stream only contains N2 and no Argon.
6.2.2
Side production costs, maintenance, insurance and labor costs
To account for the side production costs of the electrochemical O2 pump (regrouping
electricity consumption of the auxiliary systems, such as boosters or heat exchangers, the
contingency costs, the maintenance, the insurance as well as the labor costs), a yearly
operational secondary costs factor κopex secondary corresponding to 2% of the initial turnkey
price has been considered, leading to the global expression 27 of the O2 pump OPEX (in
$/kg of N2S):
OPEXO2 pump = E annual prod · elec cost + Turnkery price · 106 · κopex secondary
Total annual prod
(27)
where:
• E annual prod represents the annual energy requirements of the plant (in MWh/year),
which includes the NES production requirements as well as the purification by O2/H2
combustion energy requirements and the compression energy requirements depending
on the N2S to be produced,
• elec cost is the average electricity price over the plant lifetime (in $/MWh),
• Turnkery price is the total initial investment cost of the plant (in mil. $),
• and Total annual prod is the yearly production of Nitrogen stream (in kg/year).
This yearly operational secondary costs factor κopex secondary is considered to be
comprised 1 and 5% for usual electrochemical plants [8], but these factors
already integrate the electricity consumption. As for the electrochemical O2
pump the annual electricity consumption already represents between 3 and
4% of the initial investments, it was chosen to fix a rather low factor of 2%.
Note however that calculations were also performed with 1%, 3%, 4% and 5%, with the
corresponding results and presented in the Appendix.
Page 87 of 150
==PAGE:94==
6
TEA of the O2 pump
6.2.3
Heat requirements
For each of the three best experimental results considered for the O2 electrochemical
pump, it was investigated whether extra heat would be required or not to maintain the
temperature of the cell, and hence if it had to be included in the operational costs.
The cell heating energy requirements were evaluated considering that the feed gas and sweep
gas had to reach the desired temperature to heat up and maintain the cell temperature,
considering:
• The approximate calorific capacities of N2 and O2 as the average value of their
respective values at 300 K (26.85 ◦C) and 1100 K (826.85 ◦C): CpN2 = 1040+1187
2
=
1113.5 J.mol−1.K−1 and CpN2 = 918+1103
2
= 1010.5 J.mol−1.K−1, as well as the
calorific capacity of air Cpair = xN2 · CpN2 + xO2 · CpO2, where xN2 = 0.79 and
xO2 = 0.21 are respectively the simplified molar fractions of N2 and O2 in air.
• The air feeding gas mass flows, resulting from the mix of the N2 feed stream
ΦN2,feed in kg/s and the O2 feed stream ΦO2,feed in kg/s.
The volumetric flows used during the experiments were: 21 NL/h of N2 mixed with
5.6 NL/h of O2 for the first two tests at low utilization rate and 12.71 NL/h of N2
mixed with 3.38 NL/h of O2 for the high utilization rate test.
• The sweep gas mass flow Φsweep in kg/s: either ΦAir,sweep corresponding to a volu-
metric flow of 50 NL/h of air, or ΦO2,sweep corresponding to a volumetric flow of 50
NL/h of pure O2.
• The ASR (in Ω.cm2) of the cell measured during the experiment.
• The current I (in A) and the current density i (in A/cm2), linked by the cell active
area (Acell = 16 cm2).
• The target temperature to maintain at the center of the cell Tcent and the ambient
temperature Tamb = 20 ◦C considered as the initial temperature of the gas to be
heated.
From these assumptions and parameters, the heat ˙Qcell (in W) required to maintain a
cell center temperature Tcent could be calculated using the following equation 28, whereas
the equation 29 could be used to calculate the specific heat energy requirements Ecell (in
kWh/kg of NES):
˙Qcell = (Φsweep.Cpsweep + ΦN2,feed · CpN2 + ΦO2,feed · CpO2) · (Tcent −Tamb) (in W) (28)
Ecell =
˙Qcell
ΦNES
·
1
1000 · 3600, (in kWh/kg of NES),
(29)

where ΦNES is the mass of flow of NES produced, in kg/s.
The Joule heating or ”losses” ˙QJoule (in W) resulting from the passage of the electric
current through the cell, which can be calculated using the the Joule’s first law (eq 14),
will compensate a part of the heat requirements Ecell (in kWh/kg of NES).
The released Joule heat EJoule (in kWh/kg of NES) and the Joule heat requirements
coverage Joule heat cover (in %) can be expressed with the following equations 30:
EJoule =

”Heat requirements for the O2 pump best performances tests”
By comparing the joule losses and the cell power, one could notice that the Joule losses
are actually just a little bit higher than the power for the tests using air as sweep gas
(which is not possible, as they could only be equal in the most extreme case).
This might just be due to an approximation in the values used or errors from the measure-
ments.
However, in the test using pure O2 as sweep gas, the Joule losses only represent 74% of
the cell power...
One could also observe that with lower utilization rate, the Joule losses are reduced, due
to much smaller ASR values which results in a low coverage of the heat requirements
at low utilization rate (note that the temperature for the two low utilization rate tests
selected was 50◦C higher).
More than 90% of the theoretical heat requirements would be required to maintain the
cell temperature for the two Low UR tests, which seems too high to be reached by a heat
integration system used to heat up the ”in-gases” with the ”out-gases” calorific energy
(O2 enriched sweep gas and NES).
Page 89 of 150
==PAGE:96==
6
TEA of the O2 pump
However, the test run with high utilization rate at 700◦C shows a joule losses heat require-
ments coverage higher than 20%, implying that a heat integration system with an efficiency
close to 80% would be sufficient to maintain the cell temperature without providing
additional heat.
It was hence considered for the TEA of the electrochemical O2 pump that no pre-heater
would be necessary, allowing energy and money savings.
It was also considered that the operating costs for the heat integration system were included
in the operational secondary costs.
6.2.4
Feed and sweep gases free consideration
The air fed to the cell to become a NES was considered free, as well as the air used as
sweep gas.
The pure O2 potentially used as sweep gas could also be considered free, as one would
generate even more pure Oxygen, as O2 is extracted from the feed air and mixed with the
sweep gas.
The low utilization rate/pure O2 sweep gas test presented earlier was not considered for
the TEA for the comparison with the other technologies, but an estimation of the pure O2
production is provided here, to insist on the fact that the pure O2 production resulting
from the NES production with pure O2 as sweep gas could also be another source of benefits.
Indeed, for a feed gas resulting of the mix of 21 NL/h of N2 and 5.6 NL/h of O2, 23.26
NL/h of NES with a Nitrogen molar purity of 90.3% is produced, while the 50 NL/h
of pure O2 of sweep gas are mixed with additional 3.34 NL/h of O2, resulting from the
electrochemical O2 extraction.
Hence, not only can the pure O2 sweep gas be reused as its composition remain pure, but
the supplementary O2 stream produced could be sold.
For this particular test and produced NES composition, the pure O2 stream additional
production represents around 10% of the NES production in volume, and 16% in mass.
Knowing that gaseous N2 and gaseous O2 products have approximately the same prices
([37], ”Table 4. Prices of Raw Materials, Products, and Resources”), there might be a
balance between the over-costs linked to the higher cell potentials observed with pure O2
as sweep gas, and the benefits realized with the pure O2 side production, whereas the
O2-enriched air resulting from the use of air as sweep gas has no value.
Moreover, as mentioned in 5.5.1, operating with pure O2 rather than with air as sweep
gas at increased current densities might imply lower cell voltages obtained with the pure
Page 90 of 150
==PAGE:97==
6
TEA of the O2 pump
O2, but might come at the price of a greater and faster degradation.
The comparison of sweep gas composition at higher current densities, regarding
the cell voltages as well as the cell durability, might reveal both economic and
energy savings by using pure O2 as sweep gas.
The energy requirements of flowing the feed and the sweep gases are considered negligible
and the associated costs are assumed to be included in the side production costs.
6.3
Electrochemical O2 pump TEA energy and economic results
In this subsection, the results obtained from the electrochemical O2 Pump TEA are
presented, with a focus on the energy requirements and the influence of the investment
year and market penetration on the different LCON.
6.3.1
Energy requirements results for the production of Nitrogen Enriched
Streams by the Electrochemical O2 pump
The next Figure 27 presents the electrochemical O2 pump N2S optimal energy require-
ments obtained for the different production rates corresponding to the market penetration
discussed earlier.
The scenarios leading to the lowest energy requirements appear in the legend:
• no particular scenario for the production of NES,
• electrolyzer type for the production of pure N2S,
• electrolyzer type for the production of pure & compressed N2S.
Note that the energy requirements are steady regarding the production of NES (for
2020, 2030 and 2050), as no Hydrogen is required for this product, and hence no decrease
in the specific consumption of the electrolyzer could benefit to the electrochemical O2 pump.
Moreover, no progress was expected regarding the specific energy consumption of the avail-
able electrolyzers between 2030 and 2050, implying that the optimal energy requirements
for pure and pure & compressed N2S are equal for 2030 and 2050 investment years (and
hence overlap on the Figure).
It can also be seen that going from a NES product (with a 95.6% N2, 1.226% Ar and
3.174% O2 molar composition at 1 bar) to a purified product (with a 98.734% N2 and
1.266% O2 molar composition at 2.487 bar) using O2/H2 combustion requires around
155% of the initial energy, and a bit more than 220% if the NES product is purified and
compressed up to 150 bar.

• the production of 2.487 bar pure N2S with a 98.734% N2 and 1.266% O2 molar
composition would require:
– 0.3530 kWh/kg in 2020,
– 0.3473 kWh/kg in 2030 and 2050,
• the production of 150 bar pure N2S with a 98.734% N2 and 1.266% O2 molar

– 0.5074 kWh/kg in 2020,2030 and 2050.

It can be observed on this Figure 28 that the Capex and hence the LCON reduction
associated to an increase of the production capacities are so important for the economic
viability of the electrochemical O2 pump.
Page 94 of 150
==PAGE:101==
6
TEA of the O2 pump
Indeed, for market penetrations from 0.37 to 7402 tpd of N2 contained in the produced
NES, the LCON could represent:
• approximate relative NES LCON reductions of:
– 67% in 2020,
– 77% in 2030,
– 85% in 2050,
• approximate relative pure N2S LCON reductions of:
– 57% in 2020,
– 61% in 2030
– 69% in 2050
• approximate relative pure & 150 bar compressed N2S LCON reductions of:
– 49% in 2020
– 55% in 2030
– 62% in 2050
The reductions that an increase of the market penetration would induce are more and
more important through the years as the electricity prices are decreasing (so as the specific
energy consumption of H2), which cause the initial investment to represent a greater
portion in the LCON.
Obviously, the energy requirements are increasing when going from a production of NES,
then to the production of pure N2S and finally to the production of pure & compressed
N2S, which leads to a greater portion of the LCON to be related to energy consumption,
and smaller portions related to investment costs.
Hence, the decrease in investment costs induced by greater market penetrations has less
impact on the global LCON reduction (in %) for pure & compressed N2S than it has for
pure N2S and even less than regarding NES production.
In the Appendix, similar Figures present the electrochemical O2 pump N2S LCON obtained
with yearly operational secondary costs factor of 1% (53), 2% (54), 3% (55), 4% (56) and
5% (57) of the initial investments, as this factor couldn’t be precisely identified but yet
has a great impact on the LCON.
Thanks to these graphs, it can be noticed that this yearly operational secondary costs
factor has a greater importance (in % of LCON variation) for low market penetrations and
for NES or pure N2S production, as this annualized operational costs are simply can be
seen as just being summed up with the annualized initial investments, which, as already
Page 95 of 150
==PAGE:102==
6
TEA of the O2 pump
mentioned, represent a smaller and smaller portion of the LCON when the quality of the
product is increased (from NES to purified and purified and compressed product), or when
the production capacity increases.
6.4
Electrochemical O2 pump VS current Air Separation Tech-
nologies
In this subsection, the results obtained for the electrochemical O2 pump are compared
with the reference literature plants considered for the representation of the Air Separation
main current technologies.
6.4.1
Electrochemical O2 pump VS current Air Separation Technologies:
energy requirements
First the energy requirements of the electrochemical O2 pump are compared with the
Cryogenic, PSA and Membrane-based air separation technologies, for the production of
NES (Figure 29), the production of pure N2S (Figure 30), and the production of pure &
compressed N2S (Figure 31).
Note that the literature reference plants and the electrochemical O2 pump are represented
with two points, corresponding to 2020 or 2030/2050 investment years for the production
of pure and pure & compressed N2 (as 2030 and 2050 present the same electrolyzers
performances and compression requirements), and by only one point for the production of
NES, as the energy requirements were assumed steady over the years.
The Figure 29 shows that the electrochemical O2 pump presents higher energy needs that
other technologies which produce a less pure product (essentially membrane-based NES
production), but presents lower energy needs that other plants that produce NES with
higher purity levels, meaning that the process pretty much aligns with the current air
separation technologies.
The Figures 30 and 31 allow a more relevant comparison, for a product present more
similar quality.
From the Figure 30, it can be seen that for the production of pure N2S, the electrochemical
O2 pump presents higher energy requirements than the cryogenic plants and the PSA
plant PSA 2 (producing a 98.72%mol N2/1.38%mol Ar stream) and presents up to 1.5
times more energy that the PSA plant PSA 1 (4.2.1), but requires less energy than the
membrane-based production plants.
From the Figure 31, the energy requirements of the electrochemical O2 pump to produce
a purified and 150 bar compressed N2S are again higher than the cryogenic plants and the
two previously mentioned PSA plants.
Also note that the energy requirements would be twice higher than the energy requirements
of the PSA plant PSA 3 resulting from the simulation.

6.4.2
Electrochemical O2 pump VS current Air Separation Technologies:
LCON
Here the LCON of the electrochemical O2 pump are compared with the Cryogenic, PSA
and Membrane-based air separation technologies, for the production of NES (Figure 32),
the production of pure N2S (Figure 33), and the production of pure & compressed N2S
(Figure 34).
Note that the literature reference plants and the electrochemical O2 pump are represented
Page 99 of 150
==PAGE:106==
6
TEA of the O2 pump
with two points, corresponding to 2020, 2030 or 2050 investment years for the production
of pure and pure & compressed N2, as cheaper electricity and electrolyzers are expected
to be available over the years.
The Figure 32 does not allow a direct comparison of the plants because of the purity
disparities, but it can already be noticed that some membrane-based technologies and PSA
present higher costs than the electrochemical O2 pump for lower purity levels products.
Moreover, the electrochemical LCON are showing greater reduction over the years than
the literature plants, mainly due to the electricity price reduction expected over the
years would benefit more to the electrochemical O2 pump, which presents higher energy
consumption, as seen previously.
The Figure 33 presenting the LCON of pure N2S shows rather high results for the
electrochemical O2 pump in 2020, which couldn’t compete without a penetration of the
market of at least 1522 tpd of contained pure N2 (cf 15).
Moreover, even with the highest considered market penetration, the cryogenic plant Cryo 2
would remain much cheaper (almost twice less than the electrochemical O2 pump).
Hence the electrochemical O2 pump does not seem competitive in 2020, even though it
might present lower costs than most of the PSA and membrane-based plants.
However, in 2030, if the penetration market reaches around 1522 tpd of pure N2 contained
production (0.41% of the N2 to NH3 2020 market), only one Cryogenic plant (Cryo 2,
4.1.2) would be cheaper than the electrochemical O2 pump, and the gap between the two
technologies LCON would be even smaller for a penetration above 6087 tpd (1.6% of the
market).
In 2050, from a 1522 tpd market penetration, the electrochemical pump could show the
same LCON as the most competitive cryogenic distillation plant, and lower LCON if the
penetration of the market is even higher.
Finally, the Figure 34 could be analyzed the same way: a 2020 investment wouldn’t be
interesting as other technologies would allow the production of pure 150 bar compressed
N2S at lower costs, but for 2030 and for 2050, a sufficient market penetration of the
electrochemical O2 pump plants could lead to really competitive LCON. However, the
cryogenic distillation technology remains the cheapest, benefiting from the high pressure
of the NES and pure N2S products.

6.4.3
Extreme N2 production market penetration by the Electrochemical O2
pump technology
Considering the Table 15 providing the equivalence between cell production volume, cumu-
lative power and production rates, significant investment costs reduction can be expected
for market penetrations over 15220, 30438 and 60877 tpd of contained pure N2, which
would respectively correspond to market penetrations of penetration 4.1%, 8.2% and 16.4%.

Figure 37: Electrochemical O2 pump VS main Air Separation technologies LCON
for pure & compressed N2S production-extreme market penetration
One could observe on these Figures that even at extreme market penetrations, the
electrochemical O2 pump would still be expected to present higher LCON than the
cryogenic distillation technology in particular for the pure and pure & compressed N2S
production. However, the Cryogenic plants benefit from lower costs thanks to the great
production capacities and reduced Cape, where the electrochemical O2 pumping plants
rely on the production volume which means that greater overall production would also
benefit to electrochemical plants of reduced size and production capacity, which may be
an advantage for the implementation of medium production units at different strategic
locations
Page 106 of 150
==PAGE:113==
7
Conclusions and perspectives
7
Conclusions and perspectives
In this study, a techno-economic assessment of the three main current air separation
technologies (Cryogenic distillation, Pressure Swing Adsorption, and Membrane-based
technology) regarding the production of Nitrogen enriched stream, pure Nitrogen stream
and pure & compressed Nitrogen stream was performed considering investment in 2020,
2030 and 2050, and optimal combinations of electricity supply option in Esbjerg (Denmark)
and electrolyzer technology.
For each of the technology, the most competitive plants found in the literature were
considered to represent the global market of Nitrogen enriched streams production.
Moreover, the performances of an electrochemical O2 pumping process based on LSF/CGO
cells have been analyzed to evaluate the optimal energy requirements regarding the
production of Nitrogen enriched stream.
By considering potential industrial deployments of electrochemical Nitrogen enriched
streams production plants, it was possible to evaluate the yearly cell surface requirements
and derive investment costs. Then, by estimating the operational costs of the plants, it
was finally possible to obtain global energy requirements and costs for the production of
the different Nitrogen enriched streams from the electrochemical O2 pumping process.
In particular, the electrochemical O2 pump presents the following optimal energy require-
ments:
• 0.2287 kWh/kg regarding the production of a Nitrogen enriched stream at 1 bar and
with a molar composition of 98.07%mol N2/1.93%mol Ar,
• 0.3530 and 0.3473 kWh/kg regarding the production of a purified Nitrogen enriched
stream at 2.487 bar and with a molar composition of 98.734%mol of N2 and 1.266%mol
of Ar, respectively in 2020 and 2030/2050.
• 0.5074 kWh/kg regarding the production of a purified & 150 bar compressed Nitrogen
enriched stream at 2.487 bar and with a molar composition of 98.734%mol of N2
and 1.266%mol of Ar, in 2020, 2030 and 2050.
From an energy point of view, the electrochemical O2 pump energy results are not
competitive yet, as a few PSA plants and the cryogenic plants considered as references
show lower energy consumption regarding the production of NES, pure N2S and pure &
150 bar compressed N2S, but it can however be observed that:
• The electrochemical O2 pump would present lower LCON than most of the reference
plants even for an hypothetical investment in 2020, if the market penetration reaches
the equivalent of 1520 tpd of contained N2 to be produced, which only represents
0.4% of the 2020 N2 to NH3 market.
• In 2030, the gap with cryogenic distillation plants would reduce even more for market
penetration above 6000 tpd (between only 10% and 20% of over cost), and for a
Page 107 of 150
==PAGE:114==
7
Conclusions and perspectives
2050 investment, the electrochemical O2 pump could not only compete but represent
the cheapest technology for the production of pure N2S.
• Note however that the produced stream would still contain Argon traces, and
that regarding the production of pure & compressed N2, the cryogenic distillation
technology benefit from the high pressure of the products, and hence remains
the cheapest technology, even at high electrochemical O2 pumping plants market
penetrations.
Future analysis and optimization of the electrochemical Oxygen pumping process may lead
to lower energy requirements and costs reduction, considering that at higher utilization
rate and current densities (within the limits of degradation), the cell voltages may be lower
with pure O2 as sweep gas instead of air, which would lead to lower energy consumption,
but also allow the production and sales of supplementary pure O2 stream.
Based on the resulting competitiveness of the electrochemical process from a relatively
low hypothetical market penetration and the apparent potential of improvement of the
cells and optimization of operating conditions, further experiments and costs assessment
specific to the electrochemical O2 pumping plants should be encouraged.
